Idem. Court.
The Princes Court is mansion of the wise.
Figure of heaven, fair fountain of delights,
Theater of honors, earthly paradise,
Sudden aduancer, Sphere of purest light,
The lively Vatican of bewties bright.
Thither let Phoebus progeny resort,
Where shines their father, but in Jove's great Court?
Th. Storer. -- This is ever proper unto Courts,
That nothing can be done but fame reports.
S. Daniell. To censure is the subject of the Court,
From thence fame carries, thither fame does bring,
There too each word, a thousand echoes ring,
A Lotterie, where most loose, but few do win.
M. Drayton. Nothing in Court is done without a fee,
The Courtier needs must recompensed be.
E. Sp. Most miserable man, whom wicked fate
Has brought to Court to sue for, had I wist,
That few has found, but many one has mist.
Idem. -- The Court is counted Venus net,
Where gifts and vows, forestalls are often set:
None be so chaste as Vesta, but shall mere,
A courteous tongue, to charm her eats with sweet.
R. Green. -- The Court has much of vanity and painful ease.
W. Warner. -- The Court is now become a skittish colte,
Of wise men hardlier man aged then of the glorious dolt,
Idem. These all deformities in form in some one man we see,
More guarded then regarded, frank not to continue free,
When as the mercha^ts book, the map of all his wealth shall be.
Idem. Sometimes the courts of kings were virtuous schools,
Now find we naught in Court, but curious fools.
O you whose noble hearts cannot accord,
To be the the slaves to an infamous Lord,
And knows not how to mix with perilous art,
The deadly poison with the amorous dart,
Whose natures being found, wills no constraint,
Nor will your face with flattering pensill paint.
For we will nor woe, for pity nor for hire,
Of good my Lords their favors to require,
Go not to Court, if ye will me believe,
For in that place where ye think to relieve:
The honor due fot virtue ye shall find,
Naught but contempt which leaves good men behind.
Th. Hudson. Transl. The wanton luxury of Court,
Does form the people of like sort.
S. Daniell. Ye worthy dames that in your breasts do bear,
Of your all-seeing god, no servile fear:
Ye that of honor have a greater care,
Then sights of Courts, I pray you come not there,
Let them that in their purse have not a mite,
Cloathe them like Kings, and play the Hypocrite,
And with a lying tale and fained cheer,
Court-coozen them whom they would see on bear,
Let there the Pandar sell his wife for gain,
With service vile, his noblesse to attain:
Let him that serves the time, change his intent,
With faith unconstant sail at every vent.
Th. Hudsrn. Transl. The Court was never barren yet of those
Which could with subtle train, and hard advise,
Work on the Princes weakness and dispose,
Of feeble frailty easiest to entice.
S. Daniell. Golden cups do harbor poison,
And the greatest pomp dissembling,
Court of seasoned words has foyson
Treason haunts in most dissembling.
D. Lodge. Ye fearful wits, ye impes of Achelous,
Which wracks the wisest youth with charming voice,
Ye Circes, who by your enchauntment strange,
In stones and swine, your Lovers true do change:
Ye Stymphalids, who with your youth vptakes,
Ye Ravens that from us our riches takes.
Ye who with riches, art, and painted face,
For Priams wife puts Castor's sister in place.
Ye Myrrhaes, Canaces, and Semyrames,
And if there were yet more defamed dames,
Come all to Court, and there ye shall receive
A thousand gains, unmeet for you to have,
There shall you see the gifts of great provinces,
There shall you see the grace of graceless Princes.
Th. Hudson. Transl. Courtiers as the tide do rise and fall.
Ed. Spencer. -- It does not sit
With Courtiers majesty to be reputed
Too learned, too grave, too fine, or too conceited.
Thomas Stouer. Who full of wealth and honors blandishment,
Among great Lords his yoonger years has spent,
And quaffing deeply of the Court delights,
Used naught but tilts, armours, and maskes, and sights,
If in his age his Princes angry doom,
With deep disgrace, daine him to live at home
In homely cottage, where continually
The bitter smoke exhales abundantly,
From his before vnsorrowe-drained brain,
The brackish vapors of a silver rain,
Where usher less both day and night the North,
South East, and West winds enter and go forth.
Where round about the lower roofte-broke walls,
In stead of Arras, hang with Spider calls:
Where all at once he reaches as he stands,
With brows the roof, both walls with both his hands.
He weeps and sighs, and shunning comforts aye,
Wishes pale death a thousand times a day,
And yet at length falling to work, is glad
To bite a brown crust that the mouse has had,
And in a dish, in stead of Plate or glass,
Sups oaten drink, in stead of Hypocrasse.
I. Syluister. Courtesy.
Of Court it seems, men Courtesy do call,
For that it there most uses to abound,
And well beseems that in Princes hall,
That virtue should be plentifully found.
Which of all goodly manners is the ground,
And root of civil conversation.
Ed. Spencer. Among virtues all grows not a fairer flower,
Then is the bloome of comely Courtesy,
Which though it on a lowely stalk do bower,
Yet brauncheth forth in brave nobility,
And spreads it self through all civility:
Of which though present age do plentious seem,
Yet being matched with plain antiquity,
Ye will them all but fained shows esteem,
Which carry colors fair, which feeble eyes misdeem.
Idem. -- In the trial of true Courtesy,
Its now so far from that which once it was,
That it indeed is naught but forgerie,
Fashioned to please the eyes of them that pass,
Which see not perfect things but in a glass,
Yet is that glass so gay, it cannot blind
The wisest sight, to think that gold is brass.
But virtues seat is deep within the mind,
And not in outward show, but inward thoughts defind.
Idem. -- This noble virtue and divine,
Does chiefly make a man so rare and odd,
As in that one, they most resemble God.
S. I. Harr. Transl. -- Courteous speech usage mild and kind,
Wipes malice out of every noble mind.
S. I. Harrington. -- Courtesy ofttimes in simple bowers,
Is found as great, as in the stately towers.
Idem. It Is meet a gentle heart should ever show
By Courtesy, the fruits of true gentilitie,
Which will by practice, to an habit grow,
And make men do the same with great facility.
Likewise, the dunghill-blood a man shall know
By churlish parts, and acts of inciuilitie,
Whose nature apt to take each lewd infection,
Custom confirms, and makes ill in perfection.
Idem. Cruelty.
All lay on hands to punish Cruelty.
M. Drayton. -- Cruel deeds can never escape the scourge
Of open shame, or else some bloody death,
Repentance self, that other sins may purge,
Does fly from this, so sore the soul it sleieth,
Despair dissolues the cruel caitiffes breath,
For vengeance due does suddenly alight
On cruel deeds, the mischief to requite.
I. H. Mir. of M. Custom.
Round headed Custom th'apoplexie is,
Of bedrid nature, and lives led amiss,
And takes away all feeling of offense.
G. Chapman. Custom abusd brings virtue in disdain.
Nature with Custom joined, never fails
But by her self, and her self preuailes.
D. Lodge. Whereas to nature, forward to retain,
Lewd objects are annexed, and Customs vain,
The wounds grow desperate, and death does and,
Before good counsel can the fault amend.
Idem. Custom the worlds judgment does blind so far,
That virtue is often arraigned at vices bar.
I. Syl. Transl. Danger.
-- Danger clothed in ragged weed,
Made of bears skin, that him more dreadful, made,
Yet his own face was dreadful, ne did need
Strange horror to deforme his grisly shade,
A net in the one hand, and a rustie blade
^n the other was, this mischief, that mishap
With the one, his foes he threatened to invade,
^or whom he could not kill, he practiced to entrap.
Ed. Spencer. Danger has honor, great designs their fame.
S. Dan. The greatest dangers promise greatest bliss.
M. Drayton. Danger deuiseth shifts, wit waits on fear.
W. Shakespeare. Daunger's the chiefest joy to happiness,
And resolution honors fairest aim,
Chapter Marlowe. The path is smooth that leads unto Danger.
VV. Sh. When as we think we most in safety stand,
The greatest danger then, is near at hand.
M. Drayton. The Danger hid, the place unknown and wild,
Breeds dreadful doubts: often fire is without smoke,
And peril without show.
Ed. Spencer. Ay-me, how many perils do enfolde
The righteous man, to make him daily fall:
Were not that heavenly grace did him behold,
And steadfast truth acquit him out of all.
Idem. A thousand perils lie in close awaite,
About us daily to work our decay,
That none except a god, or god his guide,
May them avoid, or remedy provide.
Idem. In peril, we do think our selves most sure,
And often in death some men are most secure.
No Danger but in hie estate, none enuies mean degre^
VV. Warner. -- Dangerous things dissembled sildome are,
Which many eyes attend with busy care.
M. Drayton. The absent danger greater still appears,
Less fears he, who is near the thing he fears.
Most strong is he when dangers are at hand,
That lives prepard' their furies to withstand.
Of common sense he is deprived clean,
That falls with closed eyes on danger seen:
And he that may both pain and hurt eschew,
Is vain, if he his proper death pursue.
S. Daniell. Dread.
Next saw we Dread, all trembling how he shook,
With foot uncertain proffered here and there:
Benumbd of speech, and with a ghastly look,
Searched every place, all pale and dead for fear:
His cap borne up with staring of his hair.
Stoynd and amazed at his own shade for dread,
And feeling greater dangers then was need.
M. Sackuill. -- Coward Dread lackes order, fear wants art,
Deaf to attend, commanded, or defirde.
Ed. Fairfax. Transl. Death.
-- A dumb dead course we ^awe,
Heavy and cold the shape of death aright,
That daunts all earthly creatures to his laws
Against whose force in vain it i^ to fight,
Ne Peers, ne Princes, nor no mortal wight,
No Towns, ne Realms, Cities ne strongest Tower,
But all perforce must yield unto his power.
His dart anon out of his corpse he took,
And in his hand (a needful fight to see)
With great triumph eftsoons the same he shook:
That most of all my fears affraied me,
His body dight with naught but bones perdie.
The naked shape of man there saw I plain,
All save the flesh, the sinew, and the vain.
M. Sackuill. Death is a port, whereby we pass to joy,
Life is a lake, that drowneth all in pain:
Death is so near it cease all annoy.
Life is so leaud, that all it yields is vain.
And as by life to bondage man was brought,
Even so likewise by death was freedom wrought.
E. of Surrey. Naught is immortal underneath the Sun,
All things are subject to deaths tyranny:
Both clownes and kings, one selfsame course must run,
And whatsoever lives is sure to die.
Th. Kyd. Death's always ready, and our time is known
To be at heavens dispose, and not our own.
Idem. The brauest are as blossoms, and the longest liver dies:
And dead, the loueliest creature as the lothsoms carrion lies.
W. Warner. Our frailties done are written in the flowers,
Which flourish now, and fade away ere many hours.
S. Daniell. -- All earthly things be borne
To die the death, for naught long time may last:
The sun his beauty yields to winters blast.
I. H. M. of Magist. Is It not gods deed what ever thing is done,
In heaven and earth? Did not he all create
To die again? all ends that was begun:
Their times, in his eternal books of fate,
Are written sure, and have their certain date.
Who then can strive with strong necessity,
That holds the world in his still changing state?
Or shun the death ordaind by desteny,
When hour of death is come, let none ask whence or why.
Ed. Spencer. -- Death amongst all deals equally,
For he is impartial, and with one self hand
Cuts off both good and bad, none can withstand.
Chapter Middleton. Death certain is to all the proverb says:
Uncertain is to all the hour of death.
S. I. Harr. Transl. Pale fearful death with bloody dart does strike,
The wretched caitiffe and the king alike.
Untimely never comes the life's last mere,
In cradle death may rightly claim his debt,
Straight after birth, is due the fatal beer:
By deaths permission th'aged linger here.
Even in the swath-bands our commission goes,
To loose thy breath, that yet but yoongly bleweth.
I. H. Mir. of M. All music sleeps where death does lead the dance.
Ed. Spencer. Let nature for perfection mould a paragon each way,
Yet death at last on finest lumps of living flesh will pray
For nature never framed it, that never shall decay.
VV. Warner. -- Fatal death the emperor of graves.
I. Markham. Death is the key which vnlockes misery,
And lets them out to blessed liberty.
M. Drayton. All is but lost that living we bestowed,
If not well ended at our dying day.
O man have mind of that last bitter rage,
For as the tree does fall, so lies it ever low:
Ed. Spencer. No fear of death should force us to do ill.
Th. Kyd. -- When for fear of an ensuing ill
We seek to shorten our appointed race,
Then it is for fear that we our selves do kill:
So fond we are to fear the worlds disgrace.
Idem. Happy, thrice happy, who so lost his breath,
That life he gains, by his godly death.
Unwise and wretched men to weet what is good or ill,
We deem of death as doom of ill desert:
But know we fools what it us brings until:
Die would we daily once it too expert.
No danger there the shepherd can a start,
Fair fields and pleasant fields there been,
The fields aye fresh, the grass aye green.
Ed. Spencer. -- This same
Which we call death, the souls release from woe,
The work which bring our bliss to happy frame:
Sildome arrests the body, but we find
Some notice of it written in our mind.
I. Markham. The worth of all men by their end esteem,
And then due praise, or due reproach them yield.
S. Spencer. -- Death is an evil doom.
To good and bad, the common Inn of rest,
But after death the trial is to come
When best shall be to them that lived best,
But both alike when death has both suppressed.
Religious reverence does burial teene,
Which who so wants, wants so much of his rest.
For all so great shame after death I ween,
As self to dien bad, unburied, bad to been.
Ed. Spencer. -- Beasts with careless steps to laethe go,
Where men whose thoughts and honors clime on hie,
Living with fame, must learn with fame to die.
D. Lodge. Death but an acted passion does appear,
Where truth gives courage and the conscience clear.
M. Drayton. Who dies, the utmost dolor must abide:
But who that lives, is left to wail his loss,
So life is loss, and death felicity.
Sad life worse then glad death, and greater cross
To see friends grave, then dead, the grave self to engrosse.
Ed. Spencer. -- In wretches sudden death at once
There long-some ill is buried with their bones.
Th. Hudson. Transl. Death is to him that wretched life does lead
Both grace and gain; but he in hell does lie
That lives a loathed life, and wishing cannot die.
Ed. Spencer. Death is most lovely sweet and amiable:
But captived life for foulness admirable.
I. Marston. -- The toongs of dying men
Enforce attention like deep harmony,
Where words are scarce, they are sildom spent in vain:
For they breath truth, that breath their words in pain.
He that no more must say, is lissened more,
Then they whom youth and ease have taught to gloss:
More are men's ends marked, then their lives before.
The setting sun and music at the close,
As the last taste of sweet is sweetest taste,
Writ in remembrance more, then things long past.
W. Shakespeare. Delaie.
On the one side doubt, on the other sat Delaie,
Behind the gate, that none her might epsy;
Whose manner was, all passengers to stay,
And entertain with her occasions sly.
Through which, some lost great hope vnheedilie,
Which never they recover might again:
And others quite excluded forth did lie.
Long languishing there, in vnpittied pain,
And seeking often entrance afterward in vain.
Ed. Spencer. -- Danger grows by lingering till the last,
And physic has no help when life is past.
Th. Watson. -- Often things done, perhaps, do less annoy
Then may the doing, handeled with delay.
S. Daniell. Delaie in close awaite
Caught hold on me, and thought my steps to stay,
Faining still, many a fond excuse, to prate:
And time, to steal the treasure of man's day,
Whose smallest minute lost, no riches render may.
Ed. Spencer. -- Times delay new hope of help still breeds.
Idem. -- Fearful tormenting
Is leaden seruitor, to dull delay.
W. Shakespeare. He that will stop the brook must then begin
When summers heat has dried up the spring:
And when his pittering streams are low and thin*
For let the winter aid unto them bring,
He grows to be of watery floods the king:
And though you damme him up with lofty ranks,
Yet will he quickly ouerflow his banks.
R. Green. Ill news deferring, is a plague as great as an ill news.
Ab. France. Delay in love breeds doubts, but sharp denial death^
W. Shakespeare. -- Intermission suffers men dispute,
What dangers are, and cast with further care,
Cold doubt cauells with honor, scorneth fame,
And in the end fear waighes down faith with shame*
S. Daniel. Where hearts be knit, what helps if not in joy?
Delay breeds doubts, no cunning to be joy.
M. D. Delight.
In things without us, no Delight is sure^
G. Chapman. A sweet in shape is but a bad Delight.
D. Lodge. Prosperity a flatterer is found,
Delight is fearelesse till it feel the wound.
M. D. Vid. Pleasure.

Desire.
-- Desire, whom not the firmament,
Nor air, nor earth, nor Ocean can content,
Whose looks are hooks, whose bellies bottomless,
Whose hands are gripes to scrape with greedines,
Under whose command,
She brings to field a rough unruly band,
First, secret burning, mighty swollen ambition,
Whom Epicurus many worlds suffice not,
Whose furious thirst of proud aspiring dies not,
Whose hands transported with phantasticke passion,
Bear painted steeples in imaginaton.
I. Syluister. Transl. Amongst the most, the worst, we best can choose,
It Is easy to desire, but hard to use.
M. Drayton. Desire has philters, which desire procure.
Idem. If blind desire thy heart has once embraced,
Inthrall'd it is, and honor so defaced.
.. Desire with small encouraging grows bold.
M. Drayton. What can be said that Lovers cannot say?
Desire can make a Doctor in a day.
Idem. Things much retained, do make us much desire them,
And bewties seldom seen, makes us admire them.
Idem. Destiny.
Sad Clotho held the rock, the whiles the third
By grisly Lachesis, was spunne with pain,
That cruel Atropos eft-soones undid,
With cursed knife cutting the twist in twain,
Most wretched me^, whose days depe^donthrids so vain.
E. of S. The holy Prophet brought Astolpho, where
A Palace (seldom seen by mortal men)
Was placed, by which, a thick dark river ran,
Each room therein was full of diverse fleeces:
Of Wolle, of Lint of Woll, or else of Cotton,
An aged woman spunne the diverse pieces.
Whose look and hue did show her old and rotten,
Nor much unlike unto that labor this is.
By which in summer a new made silk is gotten,
Where from the silke-wormes his fine garment taking,
They reaue him of the clothes of his own making.
For first in one large room a woman span,
Infinite thrids of diverse stuff and hew,
An other does wi^h all the speed she can,
With other stuff the distaffe still renew:
The third in feature like, and pale and wan,
Seuers the fair from foul, the old from new.
Who be these here, the Duke demands his guide?
These be the fatal sisters he replide:
The Parcaes that the third of life do spin
To mortal men, hence death and nature, know
When life must end, and when it must begin.
Now she that does divide them, and bestow
The course from finer, and the thick from thin
Works to that kind, that those which finest grow,
For ornaments in Paradise must dwell.
The course are cursed, to be consumed in hell.
Further, the Duke did in the place behold,
That when the thrids were spent that had been spunne
Theit names in brass, in silver, and in gold
Were wrote, and so into great heaps were done.
From which, a man that seemed wondrous old
With whole loades of those names away did run,
And turned again as fast, the way he went,
Nor never weary was, nor never spent.
This aged man did hold his pace so swift,
As though to run, he had been only borne,
And in the lappet of his cloak were borne
The names, etc. This was time.
An heap of names within his cloak he bore,
And in the river did them all unlade:
Or to say truth, away he cast them all,
Into this stream, which Laethe we do call.
S. I. Harr. Transl. Vide. Fame.

-- You sad daughters of the quiet night,
Which in your private resolution wright,
What has, or shall upon our fortunes light,
Whose stories none may see, much less recite;
You rulers of the Gods.
I. Markham. Down in the bottom of the deep Abisse,
Where Demogorgon in dull darkness p^nt,
Far from the view of Gods, or heavens bliss,
The hidious Chaos, there dreadful dwelling is.
Ed. Spencer. What man can turn the stream of Destenie?
Or break the chain of strong necessity?
Which fast is tide to Jones eternal seat?
Idem. -- What shall be shall. There is no choice,
Things needs must drive as Destenie decrees:
For which we ought in all our haps rejoice,
Because the eye eternal, things foreseeth:
Which to no ill at any time agrees,
For ills, too ill to us, be good to it,
So far his skill exceeds, our reach of wit.
I. H. Mir. of M. Woe worth the wight that strives with Gods foresight
They are not wise, but wickedly do err,
Which think ill deeds due destenies may bar.
Idem. No humble speech nor moan, may move the fixed stint.
Of Destiny or death: such is the will that paints
The earth with colors fresh, ye darkish skies with store
Of Starry light.
Ed. Spencer. Walls may a while keep out an enemy,
But never castle kept out destiny.
M. Drayton. -- Who can deceive his destiny?
Or ween by warning to avoid his fate?
That when he sleeps in most security,
And safest seems, him soonest does amate,
And finds due effect, or soon, or late,
So feeble is the power of fleshly arm.
Ed. Spencer. -- That which Jove and Destiny have done,
Men may lament, but never disanull.
Chapter Fitz. vide fate.

Despair.
Ere long they came where that same wicked wight
His dwelling has, low in a hollow Cave,
Far underneath a craggy clift upright,
Dark, doleful, drery, like a drery grave,
That still for carrion carcasses does crave.
On top whereof, aye dwells the ghastly Owl,
Shriking his balefull note, which ever drove
Far from that haunt, all other cheerful foul.
And all about it wandering ghostes do wail and houle*
And all about old stocks and stubs of trees,
Whereas, nor fruit, nor leaf was ever seen,
Did hang upon the ragged rocky trees,
On which had many witches hanged been,
Whose carcasses were scattered on the green,
And thrown about the cliffes.
Ed. Spencer. That darkesome cave they enter, where they find
That cursed man, low sitting on the ground,
Musing full sadly in his sullen mind:
Hi^ grisly locks, long grown and unbound,
Disordered hung about his shoulders round
And hid his face; through which his hollow eyes
Looked deadly, dull, and stared as astound.
His rawebone cheeks through penury and pine,
Were shrunk into his jaws, as he did never dine,
His garment naught but many ragged clouts,
With thorns together pind and patched was,
The which his naked sides he wraps abouts.
And him beside, there lay upon the grass,
Adrery coarse, whose life away did pass,
All wallowed in his own, yet lukewarm blood,
That from his wound yet welled fresh a less.
In which a rustie knife fast fixed stood,
And made an open passage for the gushing flood.
Idem. Me thought by night, a grisly ghost in dark I saw,
Eke ever still to me with stealing steps she drew,
^he was of color pale, and deadly hew,
Her clothes resembled thousand kinds of thrall,
And pictures plain of hastened deaths withal.
I. H. Mir. of M. -- Despair
The factor for improuident restraint.
I. Markeham. -- Despair, that deep disdained elfe,
Delightlesse lives, still stabbing of her self.
D. Lodge. -- As it is not lawful for a man
At such a Kings departure or decease,
To leave the place, and falsify his faith,
So in this place we ought not to surrender
That deerer part, till heaven it self command it.
For as they lent us life to do us pleasure,
So look they for return of such a treasure.
Th. Kyd. Far greater folly is it for to kill
Themselves dispairing, then is any ill.
I. H. M. of M. Be resolute, not desperate, the Gods that made thee poor,
Can if they will (do wait their will) thy former state rest^^^
W. Warner. -- When last need to desperation drives
Who dares the most, wisest counsel gives.
S. I. Harrington. We may in war sometime take truce with foes,
But in Despair, we cannot with our woes.
M. Drayton. Despair has ever danger all contemned.
Idem Devil.
Hells prince, sly parent of reuolts, and lies.
I. Syluister. O ruthlesse murderer of immortal souls,
A less, to pull us from the happy poales,
And plunge us headlong in the yawning hell,
Thy ceaselesse frauds and fetches who can tell?
Thou play'st the Lion when thou do engage,
Blood-thirstie Neroes barbarous heart with rage,
While flesht in murders, butcherlike he paints
The Saint-poore world, with the dear blood of Saints*
Thou plaiest the dog, when by the mouth profane,
Of some false Prophets thou do belch thy bane.
Where from the Pulpet barkingly he rings,
Bold blasphemies against the King of Kings.
Thou plai'st the swine when plunged in pleasures vile,
Some Epicure does sober minds defile,
Transforming lewdly by his loose impiety,
Sweet Lacedemon to a soft society.
Thou plaiest the Nightingale, or else the swan,
When any famous Rhetorician
With captious wit, and curious language draws,
Seduced hearers, and subuerts the laws.
Thou play'st the fox when thou do feign aright.
The face and phrase of some deep Hypocrite.
True painted tomb, dead seeming cole, but quick,
A scorpion fell, whose hidden tail does prick:
Yet this were little, if thy spite audacious,
Spared (at the least) the face of angels gracious,
And if thou did not apely immitate
The Almighty's works, the wariest wits to mate.
I. Syl. Transl. The ghostly enemy does not stay,
Till tempted persons do obey.
Yield to him, he a Lion is,
Gain stood a fly, his pray does mis.
Ignoto. A subtle Pandar with more enticing rights,
Then sea has fish, or heaven has twinkling lights.
I. Syl. As a false Lover that thick snares has laid,
To entrap the honor of a fair young maid,
When she (though listening) little ear affords,
To his sweet courting deep affected words,
Fears some asswaging of his freezing flame,
And soothes himself with hope to gain his game,
And wrapped with joy upon this point persists,
That parlying city never long resists.
Even so the serpent that does counterfeit
A guilefull call to allure us to his net:
Perceiving Eve his flattering gloze digest,
He prosecutes, and iocund does not rest,
Till he have tried foot, hand, head and all,
Upon the breach of this new battered wall.
I. Syl. Transl. Discord.
Hard by the gates of hell her dwelling is,
There where as all the plagues and harms abound*
Which punish wicked men that walk amiss.
It is a darkesome delue, far under ground,
And thorns which barren brooks inuirond roun^
That none the same may easily out win,
Yet many ways to enter may be found,
But none to issue forth when one is in,
For Discord harder is to end then to begin.
Ed. Spencer. Ate, mother of debate
And all dissension which does daily grow
Amongst frail men, that many a public state
And many a private often it does ore^unne.
Idem. -- He knew her weed of sundry hew,
Patched with infinite unequal lists,
Her skin in sundry naked places view,
At diverse rents and cuts he may that lists:
Her hair was gray, and red, and black and blew,
And hard and soft, in laces some she twists:
Some hangs down, upright some stands staring,
As if each hair with other had been squaring.
Her lap was full of writs and of citations,
Of processes, of actions, and arrests,
Of bills, of answers, and of replications,
Greeuing the simple sort with great vexations.
She had resorting to her as her guests
Attending on her circuits and her iournies,
Suitors and Clerks, Lawiers, and Atturnies.
S. I. Harr. Transl. Her face most foul and filthy was to see,
With squinted eyes contrariwise intended:
And loathly mouthed, unmeet a mouth to be.
That naught but gall and venom comprehended,
and wicked words that God and man offended.
Her lying tongue was in two parts divided,
and both the parts did speak, and both contended:
and as her tongue so was her heart descided.
That never thought on them, but doubly still was guided
Ed. Spencer. All like as drops engender mighty floods,
And little seeds sprout forth great leaves and buds:
Even so small strifes if they be suffered run,
Breed wrath and war, and death ere they be done.
M. of Magist. Concord in kingdoms is great assurance,
And royal fame lies do never fall:
But where discord does lead the doubtful dance,
With busy brawles, and terms of variance,
Where malice minstrell is, the pipe ill report,
The mask mischief, and so does end the sport.,
Idem. Firebrand of hell first tinde in Phlegeton,
By thousand suries, and from thence outthrowne
Into this world, to work confusion,
And set it all on fire by force unknown,
Is wicked discord; whose small sparks once blown,
None but a God or godlike man can slake:
Such as was Orpheus, that when strife was grown
Amongst those famous Imps of Greece, did take
His silver harp in hand, and shortly friends them mak^
Ed. Spencer. O cruel discord, food of deadly hate,
O mortal corsiue to a commonweal:
Death-lingring consumption to a state,
A poisoned sore that never salve could heal.
O soul contagion, deadly killing fever,
Infecting often, but ^o be cured never.
M. Drayton. A state divided, cannot firmly stand:
Two Kings within one realm could never rest.
T. Kyd. Dissimulation.
-- Fierce lightening from her eyes
Did set on fire fair Heroes sacrifice:
Which was her torn robe and enforced hair,
And the bright flame became a maid most fair
For her aspect; her tresses were of wire,
Knit like a net, where hearts all set on fire,
Struggled in pants and could not get releast:
Her arms were all with golden pincers dressed,
And twenty fashioned knots, pullies and brakes,
And all her body girdled with printed snakes.
Her down parts in a scorpion tail combinde,
Freckled with twenty colors p^edwings shinde
Out of her shoulders; cloth did never die,
Nor sweeter colors ever viewed eye.
In scorching Turkey, Cares, Tartarie:
Then shinde about this spirit notorious,
Nor was Arachnes web so glorious.
Of lightning and of shreds she was begot,
More hold in base dissemblers is there not.
Her name was Eronusis.
G. Chapman. The colors of dissemblance and deceit,
Were died deep in grain, to seem like truth.
Ed. Spencer. Better a wretch then a dissembler.
E. Gilpin. -- Commonly in dissimulations
The Excess of glauering does guile ^^tect,
Reason refuses falsehood to direct.
The will therefore for fear of being spied,
Exceeds mean, because it wants guide.
M. of M. -- Commonly all that counterfeit
In any thing, exceed the natural mean,
And that for fear of fa^^ing in their feat.
Idem. The lovely looks, the sighs that storm so sore,
The due of deep dissembling doubleness:
These may attempt, but are of power no more,
Where beauty leanes to wit and soothfastnesse.
D. Lodge. -- Who has to do
With deep dissemblers, must dissemble too.
Chapter Middleton. Vid. Hypocrisy.

End.
-- The End does always prove the fact,
By End we judge the meaning of the act.
S. I. H. Transl. Begin where lightness will, in shame it ends.
G. Chapman. Earth.
Thus whilst he laid his head upon her lap,
She in a fiery mantle does him wrap:
And carries him up from his lumpish mould,
Into the skies whereas he might behold
The Earth in perfect roundnesse of a ball,
Exceeding globes most artificial.
Which in a fixed point nature disposed,
And with the sundry elements enclosed.
Which as the Center, permanent does stay,
When as the skies in their diurnall sway:
Strongly maintain the euerturning course,
Forced alone by their first mouers source.
Where he beholds the aerie regions,
Whereas the clouds and strange impressions
Maintaind by coldness often do appear:
And by the highest region of the air
Unto the clearest element of fire,
Which to her silver footstool does appear.
M. Drayton. The Moon is darkened to all creatures eyes,
Whilst in the shadow of the earth she lies:
For that the earth of nature cold and dry,
A very Chaos of obscurity:
Whose globe exceeds her compass by degrees,
Fixed upon her superficies.
When in his shadow she does hap to fall,
Does cause her darkness to be general.
Idem. -- Earth
Bears all her sons and daughters in one womb,
She Europes, Ameriques, Affriques, Asians toombe.
Idem. -- Earth cannot comprehend
The secret depths of judgments all divine,
Where is no ground beginning, midst nor fine.
I. Syluester. Transl. O trustlesse state of earthly things, and slipper hope
Of mortal men, that swinke and work for naught:
And shooting wide, does miss the marked scope.
Now have I turned (a lesson dearly bought)
That nis on earth assurance to be sought.
Ed. Spencer. A narrow room our glory vain unties,
A little circle does our pride contain:
Earth like an I Will amid the water lies,
Which sea sometime is called, sometime the maine.
Yet naught therein resounds a name so great,
It Is but a lake, a pond, a marish street.
Ed. Fairfax. Transl. Our mother earth nere glories in her fruit,
Till by the sun clad in her tinsell suit:
Nor does she ever stare him in the face,
Till in her glorious arms she him embrace.
Which proves she has a soul, sense, and delight,
Of generation, feeling, appetite.
M. Drayton. To know our selves to come of humane birth,
These sad afflictions cross us here on earth.
A task imposde by heavens eternal law,
To keep our rude rebellions well in awe.
M. Drayton. Next unto him, malicious Envy rode,
Upon a ravenous Wolf, and still did chawe
Between his cankred teeth a venomous tode,
That all the poison ran about his iawe.
But inwardly he chawed his own mawe
At neighbors wealth, that made him ever sad,
For death it was when any good he saw,
And wept, that cause of weeping none he had,
But when he heard of harm, he waxed wondrous glad.
Ed. Spencer. The other held a snake with venom fraught,
On which she fed and gnawed hungerly:
As if that long she had not eaten ought,
That round about the jaws we might discry
The bloody gore, and poison dropping loathsomly.
Her name was Envy, known well thereby,
Whose nature is to grieve and grudge at all
That she sees done praise worthily:
Whose sight to her is greatest cross may fall,
And vexes so, that makes her eat her gall:
For when she wants other thing to eat,
She feeds on her own man unnatural.
And of her own foul entrails make her meat,
Meat fit for such a monsters monstrous diet.
Idem. I chanced on a monster of a man,
With health heart sick, sterued with store of food,
With riches poor, with beauty pale and wan,
Wretched with happiness, evil with good.
One eye did envy at the the other eye:
Because the other enuide more then he,
His hands did fight for the first injury,
So Envy enuide, enuide to be.
And as he went, his tender foot was sore,
And enuide at the foot that went before.
Th. Bastard. This monster honors hurt, is like the curre
That barks at strangers coming at the durre.
But sparing always those are to her known,
To them most gentle, to the others thrown.
This monster als is like a rauing cloud,
Which threatens always kindly Vulcan loud
To smore and drown him with her pouring rain,
Yet force of fire repels his force again.
K. of S. Often malice makes the mind to shed the boiled brine,
And enuies humor often vnlades, by conduits of the eine.
T. W. Envy lives with us whilst our selves survive,
But when we die, it is no more alive.
Chapter Fitz Ieffry. The knottie Oak and wainscot old,
Within does eat the silly worm,
Even so a mind in envy cold,
Always within it self does burn.
Idem. Each sense may common subjects comprehend,
Things excellent the sensitive confound:
The eye with light and colors may contend:
The care endure the note of common sound
Both fail, when glorious beams and strokes abound*
So Envy that at meanest things bears spite,
Stands mute at view of unexpected height.
Th. Storer. -- Envy harbors most in feeblest breast.
S. Phil. Sidney. Fell enuies cloud, still dimmeth virtues ray.
Ed. Spencer. Foul envy, thou the partial judge of right,
Son of deceit, borne of that harlot hate:
Nursed in hell, a vile and ugly sprite,
Feeding on slander, cherisht with debate,
Never contented with thine own estate:
Deeming alike, the wicked and the good,
Whose words be gall, whose actions end in blood.
M. Drayton. Envy does cease, wanting to feed upon.
Idem. Like as the poyze that would the palm repress
Does cause the bows spread larger round about:
So spite and envy causes glory sprout,
And aye the more the top is ouertroad,
The deeper does the same root spread abroad.
M. of M. Sicilian Tyrants yet did never find
Then Envy, greater torment of the mind.
Idem. Our days are stamped in Enuies mint,
And this our age cast in the Iron mold,
Our hearts are hewed out of Cancasean flint,
And two leau'd plates of brass our breast enfold,
Hate waxes young, the world thus waxing old,
And best we like them, that do us love the least,
And least we love them, whom we should like best.
Chapter Fitz Geffrey. Error.
-- His gliste^ing armor made
A little glooming light, much like a shade:
By which she saw the ugly monster plain,
Half like a serpent, horribly displaied,
But the other half did woman's shape retain,
Most loathsome, filthy, foul, and full of vile disdain.
And as she lay upon the dyrtie ground,
Her huge long tail, her den all ouerspred,
Yet was in knots, and many bouts vpwound:
Pointed with mortal sting: of her there bred
A thousand young ones, which she daily fed,
Sucking upon her poisoned dugges, each one
Of sundry shapes, yet all ill sauoured.
Soon as the uncouth light upon them shone,
Into her mouth they crept, and suddenly were gone.
Ed. Spencer. To err is proper unto men, and but brutish to persist*
W. Warner. -- Errors are no errors, but by fate,
For often the event makes foul faults fortunate.
S. Daniell. -- Errors left unpunished, are professed,
And being not defended, are oppressed.
Chapter Middleton. To hear good counsel, error never loves.
D. Lodge. -- Errors are hardly moved,
That love does breed in an unadvised breast.
S. I. H. Transl. A stony coldness has benumbde the sense,
And lively spirits of each living wight,
And dimmed with darkness their intelligence,
Darkness more then Cymerians day by night,
And monsters Error flying in the air,
Has marred the face of all that seems fair.
Ed. Spencer. Faith.
-- Fidelia
Like sunny beams threw forth her crystal face,
That could have mazde the r^r'st beholders sight,
And round about her head did shine like heavens light
She was araied all in Lilly white,
And in her right hand bare a Cup of Gold,
With wine and water filld up to the height.
In which a serpent did himself infold,
That horror made to all that did behold,
But she nowhit did change her constant mind.
And in her other hand, she full did hold,
A book that was both signd and sealed with blood.
Ed. Spencer. Faith sits triumphant on a coach of gold,
Of Tuballs work, where costly Saphires shine,
Rich Diamonds, and many Rubies fine,
And if ought else, the work more costly hold,
This glorious chariots rolling wheels are like
The holy wheels the great Ezechiel saw,
For own self spirit, self wind and will does draw,
Their restesse courses equal both alike,
The bird that led the Roman standerds out
The bird that fixed can oppose his eyes,
Against the greatest light in all the skies,
High through the air draws this rich Coach about.
Faith flaunts it not in silver nor in gold.
Nor precious scarlet of the Tyrian die:
Nor paints her face to hide deformity,
But as she is, she does her self unfold,
Her body that all bodies does disgrace,
Like Iunoes bird is full of watching eyes,
Whose holy glaunces pierce the lofty skies,
Pierce the hie heavens, and see God face to face.
She has great store of flowing tongues to praise
The Lord of hoastes: she has most mighty wings,
(Passing the swiftnes of all mortal things)
That in a moment up to heaven her raise,
Her glorious head is compast with a crown
Nor made of Olive, pine, or Laurel bow,
Nor Parsley wreath which Graecians did allow.
The olympian gaimes for signalls of renown,
But of fresh Roses plucked from honors tree,
That never shrink for winters chilling frost,
Nor wither not when Titan hotely tostes,
But by the Lord for ever watered be.
I. Syl. Transl. Faith friendly porter of heavens Crystal hold,
Conduct us straight before the throne of gold:
O^ Gods great grace, there prostrate on her knee,
Does prayer speak in name of all the three.
I. S. Transl. What was the world before the world? or God ere he was God
Why this he did, or does not that, this bidde^ or forbod:
I dare not think, or arrogate such mysteries divine,
Faith with her wits significant suffice these wits of mine:
To love God and our neighbors as our self is all in fine.
W. Warner. Draw thy forces all unto thy heart,
The strongest fortress of this earthly part:
And on these three, let thy assurance lie,
On faith, hope, and humility.
M. Drayton. Faith is thy Fort, thy shield, thy stronger aid,
Never controlled, nere yielded, ne dismaide:
Which does dilate, unfold, foretell, expresses,
Which gives rewards, inuesteth and possesses.
Idem. Faith has not only power on things terrene
Both hie, and low, but oftentimes does force
Gods justice too, and sometimes seems perforce,
Gods purposes to change and alter clean.
I. Syl. -- The hardest things faith makes most possible.
Idem. -- Even the faithful flocks are like the ground,
That for good fruit, with weeds will still abound:
If that the share and coulter idle lie,
That riues the share, and roots the brambles bie.
Th. Hudson. Transl. Add faith unto your force, and be not faint.
Ed. Spencer. Only faith does justify say we, of Gods free grace,
By Christ, nor faith is idle, but does charity embrace.
W. Warner. Fame.
A monster swifter none is under sun.
Increasing as in waters we discrie,
The circles small of nothing that begin,
Which at the length, unto such breadth do come,
That of a drop which from the skies do fall,
The circles spread, and hide the waters all.
So Fame in flight increasing more and more,
For at the first, she is not scarcely known,
But by and by she flits from shore to shore,
To clouds from the earth, her stature straight is grown
There whatsoever by her trumpe is blown,
The sound that both by sea and land out-flies,
Rebounds again, and verberates the skies:
They say, the earth that first the Giants bred,
For anger that the Gods did them dispatch,
Brought forth this sister of those monsters dead,
Full light of foot, swift wings, the winds to catch,
Such monster erst did nature never hatch.
As many plumes she has as top to toe,
So many eyes them underneath or more:
And tongues do speak: so many ears do hark,
By night tweene heaven she flies and earthly shade,
And shreaking takes no quiet sleep by dark,
On houses roofes, or towers as keeper made,
She fits by day, and cities threats to invade,
And as she tells what things she sees by view,
She rather shows that is fained false, then true,
I. H. Mir. of M. Fame in a stoale of purple set with eyes,
And ears, and tongues, carried a golden book,
Upon the cover, this I saw engrau'd.
Pauci quos aequus amauit
Jupiter, aut ardeus euerit ad aethera virtus
Dijs geniti.
G. Peele. Fame with golden wings aloft does fly,
Above the reach of ruinous decay,
And with brave plumes does beat the ayrie sky,
Admired of base borne men, from far away.
Ed. Spencer. The Brazen Trompe of Iron winged fame,
That mingleth truth with forged lies.
E. Fairfax. Transl. Then came they to the foul and loathsome lake,
Dark, deep, and mirie, of a dreadful hue,
Where was the aged man that never stinted
To carry bundles of the names imprinted.
This was the man, whom (as I told before)
Nature and custom so swift of foot had made,
He never rested, but ran evermore.
And with his coming he did use this trade:
A heap of names within his cloak he bare,
And in the River did them all unlade:
Or to (say truth) away he cast them all,
Into this stream which Laethe we do call.
This prodigal old wrerch no sooner came
Unto this cursed rivers barren bank,
But desperately without all fear of blame,
Or caring to deserve reward or thank,
He hurled therein full many a precious name,
Where millions soon into the bottom sanke:
Hardly in every thousand one was found,
That was not in the gulf quite lost and dround:
Yet all about great store of birds there flew,
As vultures, carren crows, and chattering pies,
And many more of sundry kinds and hew,
Making leaude harmony with their loud cries,
These when the careless wretch the treasure threw
Into the stream, did all they could devise,
What with their tallents some, and some with beak
To save these names, but find themselves too weak.
For ever as they thought themselves to raise,
To bear away those names of good renown,
The weight of them, so heavy downward ways,
They in the stream were driven to cast them down,
Only two swans sustained so great a paize
In spite of him that sought them all to drown,
These two did still take up whose names they list,
And bare them safe away, and never mist.
Sometime all under the foul lake they diued,
And took up some that were with water covered:
And those that seemed condemned, they repriued.
And often as about the bank they houered,
They caught them, ere they to the stream arrived,
Then went they with the names they had recovered,
Up to a hill that stood the water nie,
On which a stately Church was built on hie.
This place is sacred to immortal fame,
And evermore a Nymph stands at the gate,
And took the names wherewith the two swans came,
Whether they early come, or whether late.
Then all about the Church she hanged the same,
Before that sacred Image in such rate,
As they might then well be assured for ever,
Spite of that wretch, in safety to persevere.
S. I. Harr. Transl. Fame on his right hand in a roabe of gold,
Whose stately train, Time as her page did bear:
On which for rich imbroydery was enrold,
The deeds of all the Worthies ever were:
So strongly wrought as wrong could not impair,
Whose large memorialls she did still rehearse,
In Poets man immortallizing verse.
Two tablets on her goodly breast she bore,
The one of Crystal, the other Ebonie,
Engrau'd with names of all that lived before
That; the fair book of heavenly memory:
The Other, the base scrowle of Infamy.
One stuffed with Poets, Saints, and Conquerors,
The Other, with Atheists, Tyrants, Usurers,
And in her word appeared as a wonder,
Her daring force, and never failing might:
Which softly spoke far off, as't were a thunder,
And round about the world would take their flight,
And bring the most obscured things to light.
That still the farther off, the greater still,
Did ever sound our good, or make our ill.
M. Drayton. Her dwelling is betwixt the earth and skies,
Her Turret unto heaven her top vpreares:
The windows made of Lynceus piercing eyes,
And all the walls be made of daintiest ears,
Where every thing that is done in earth appears.
No word is whispered in this vaultie round,
But in her palace straitwaies it does sound:
The rafters, trumpets which do rend the air,
Sounding aloud each name that thither comes.
The chinkes like tongues of all things talking here,
And all things past, in memory do bear.
The doors unlock with every word man says,
And opens wide with every little breath.
It Is hung about with arms and conquering spoils,
The pillars which support the roof of this,
Are trophies graven with Herculean toiles.
The roof of garlands, crown, and ensigns is:
In midst of which a Crystal Pyramid,
All over carved with men of most renown,
Whose base is her fair chair, the spire her crown.
Idem. -- Fame
Refuge of hope, the harbinger of truth,
Hand-mayd of heaven virtues skillful guide,
The life of life, the ages springing youth:
Triumph of joy, eternities fair bride,
The virgins glory, and the martyrs pride.
The courages immortal raising fire,
The very height to which great thoughts aspire:
The staire by which men to the stars do clime:
The minds first mouer greatness to express,
Faiths armor, and the vanquisher of time.
A pleasant sweet against deaths bitterness,
The hie reward which does all labors bless:
The study which does heavenly things impart,
The joy amidst the tedious ways of art.
Learnings green laurel, Justice glorious throne,
The Muses chariot, memories true food:
The Poets life, the gods companion,
The fire-reuiuing Phaenix sun-nurst brood.
The spirits eternal image, honors good.
The Balsamum which cures the soldiers scarres,
The world, discovering seamens happy stars.
Idem. A lofty subject of it self does bring,
Grave words and weighty, of it self divine:
And makes the authors holy honor shine.
If ye would after ashes live, beware:
To do like Erostrate, who burnt the fair
Ephesian Temple, or to win a name
To make of brass a cruel calfe vntame.
K. of S. -- Incorporeall Fame
Whose weight consists in nothing but her name,
Is swifter then the wind, whose tardy plumes
Are reeking water, and dull earthly fumes.
Chapter Marlowe. Fame (whereof the world seems to make such choice)
Is but an Echo and an idle voice.
S. Daniell. Unto this Hydra are we subject still,
Who dares to speak, not caring good or ill.
Better it is without renown to be,
Then be renownd for vile iniquity.
K. of K. -- Fame the queen of immortality.
Chapter Fitz Ieffrey. Death has no dart to slay deserved Fame.
Chapter Fitz. This jealous monster has a thousand eyes,
Her aerie body has a thousand wings:
Now on the earth, now up to heaven she flies.
And here and there with every wind she flings:
Nothing so secret but to her appears,
And apt to credit every thing she hears.
Foul babbling, tell tale, secrets soon bewraier,
The air bred Echo, the speaker of lies:
Shrill-sounding trompet, truths unkind betraier.
False larum-bell, awaking dead men's eyes.
Fond prattling parrat telling all thou hear,
Often furthest off, when as thou should be nearest.
M. Drayton. The path is set with danger, leads to fame,
When Minos did the Grecians flight deny,
He made him wings and mounted through the sky.
Idem. Still fame will grow if once abroad it fly,
Whether it be a troth, or be a lie.
Idem. Fame does explore what lies most secret hidden,
Entering the closet of the palace dweller,
A broad revealing what i^ forbidden,
Of truth and falsehood both an equal teller,
It Is not a guard can serve for to expel her:
The sword of justice cannot cut her wings,
Nor stop her mouth from uttering secret things.
S. Daniell. Celestial goddess everliving fame,
Mineruaes daughter by fair Maias son,
Of all the inhabitants of heavens fair frame:
Most highly honored since the world begun,
And shall be till the fatal glass be run.
Souls sweet receipt, the healths restoratiue:
Hearts cordial, the minds preservative.
Goddess of thoughts, muse animating appetite,
Altar of honor, simple of renown,
Shrine of devotion, yielding art her merit:
Life's richest treasure, virtues gorgious gown,
Heavens best abilliment, Ariadnes crown.
The Cynosura of the purest thought,
Fair Helice, by whom the heart is taught.
Chapter Fitz Ieffrey. Famine.
A grisly shape of Famine might we see,
With greedy looks and gaping mouth that cride
And would torment as she should there have did:
Her body thin and bare as any bone,
Whereto was left naught but the case alone:
And that alas was gnawne on every where,
All full of holes, that I ne might refrain
From tears to see how she her arms could tear,
And with her teeth gnash on her bones in vain,
When all for naught she feign would so sustain
Her staruen corpse, that rather seemed a shade,
Then any substance of a creature made.
Great was her force, whom stone walls could not stay,
Her tearing nails snatching at all she saw:
With gaping jaws, that by no means y may
Be satisfied from hunger of her mawe,
But eats her self, as she that has no law:
Gnawing alas her carkas all in vain,
While you may count each sinew, bone and vain:
On her, while we thus firmly fixed our eye,
That bled for ^uth of such a drery sight,
Lo suddenly she shrikte in so huge wise,
As made hell gates to shiuer with the might:
Where with a dart we saw how it did light
Right on his breast, and therewithal pale death
Enthrilling it, to reaue her of her breath.

Fancy we feel includes all passions might.

Fate.
What God has said, that cannot but ensue,
Though all the world would have it overthrown:
When men suppose by fetches of their own
To fly their Fate, they further on the same,
Like blasts of wind, which often revive the flame.
M. of M. The heavens do rule in their continual course,
That yields to Fate, that does not yield to force.
M. Drayton. Chance is uncertain, fortune double faced.

R. Green. -- The Fates can make
Way for themselves, their purpose to partake.

Not all the gods can change, nor Jove himself can free.
Idem. -- The laws of Fate
Being grau'n in steel, must stand inuiolate.

Ed. Spencer. -- Who can deceive his destenie?
Or ween by warning to avoid his Fate?
That when he sleeps in more security
And safest seems, him soonest does amate,
And finds due effect, or soon or late
So feeble is the power of fleshly arm.
Idem. -- Indeed the Fates are firm,
And may not shrink though all the word do shake:

And guide the heavenly causes to their constant term.
Idem. Each man they say his Fate has in his hands,

M. Drayton. Fear.
-- Fear all armed from top to toe,
Yet thought himself not safe enough thereby,
But feared each shadow moving too and fro,
And his own arms whom glistering he did spy,
Or clashing heard, he fast away did fly
As ashes pale of hew, and winghie heeld,
And evermore on danger fixed his eye,
Against whom he always bent a brazen sheeld,
Which his right hand unarmed faithfully did weeld.
Ed. Spencer. Who so for fickle fear from virtue shrinkes,
Shall in his life embrace no worthy thing,
No mortal man the cup of surety drinks.
S. Phil. Sid. Fear is more pain then is the pain it fears,
Disarming humane minds of native might:
Where each conceit an ugly figure bears,
Which were not evil, well viewd in reasons light.
Idem. The gift being great, the fear does still exceed,
And extreme fear can neither fight nor fly,
But cowardlike with trembling terror die.
W. Shakespeare. The fear of ill, exceeds the ill we fear.
Ed. Fairfax. Transl. Fear lends wings to aged folk to fly,
And made them mount to places that were hie.
Fear made the woeful child to wail and weep,
For want of speed, on foot and hands to creep.
Th. Hudson. Transl. Fear in a fearful heart, frets more then plagues that
he fears.
A. France. Fear that is wiser then the truth does ill.
S. D. Fear casts too deep, and never is too wise.
Idem. In vain with terror is he fortified,
That is not guarded with firm love beside.
Idem. A fearful thing to tumble from a throne.
M. Drayton. Where crowned might, and crossed right so near together dwell
Behoues that forest flying fear whereof the Fox does tell,
Our factious Lancaster and York, thereof could witness well.
VV. Warner. Thunder affrights the Infants in the schools,
And threatenings are the conquerors of fools.
I. Markham. Whom fear constraines to praise their Princes deeds,
That fear eternal, hatted in them feeds.
R. Green. Fear misinterprets things, each angury
The worser way he fondly does imply.
Weakness is false, and faith in cowards rare,
Fear finds our shifts, timitidie is subtle.
S. Daniell. It Is incident to those whom many fear,
Many to them more grievous hate to bear.
M. Drayton. -- He whom all men fear,
Fears all men every where:
(Hate enforcing them thereto)
Makes many undertake
Many things they would not do.
Th. Kyd. The only good that grows of passed fear,
Is to be wise, and ware of like again.
Ed. Spencer. A man to fear a woman's moodie eire,
Makes reason lie a slave to servile fear.
S. Ph. Sydney. Nothing seen fearful, we the most should fear,
Great amistes rise before the greatest rain:
The water deep'st, where we least murmr hear,
In fairest Cups men temper deadliest baine.
The nearer night, the air more clear and still,
The nearer to one deaths, least fearing ill.
M. Drayton. -- Bloodlesse, trustlesse, witless fear,
That like an Aspen tree, trembles each where,
She leads black terror, and black clownish shame,
And drowsy sloth that counterfeiteth lame,
With snailelike motion measuring the ground.
Foul sluggish drone, barren (but sin to breed)
Diseased beggar, steru'd with wilful need.
I. S. Transl. The fear of evil does affright us more,
Then the evil it self, though it seem nere so sore.
Fortitude.
Rich buskind Seneca, that did declaime
And first in Rome our tragic pomp compile,
Says Fortitude is that, which in extreme,
And certain hazard all base fear exile.
It guides says he, the noble mind from far,
Through frost and fire, to conquer honors war.
I. Markham. Honey tong'd Tully Marmaid of our ears,
Affirms, no force can force true Fortitude:
It with out bodies no communion bears.
The soul and spirit, soly it does include.
It is that part of honesty, which reares
The heart to heaven, and ever does obtrude,
Faint fear and doubt, still taking his delight
In pe^ills, which exceed all perils might.
Patience, perseverance, greatness, and strong trust,
These Pages are to Fortitude their King:
Patience that suffers, and esteems just,
What ever we for virtue fortunes brine.
Perseverance holds constant what we must,
Greatness that effects the gilded thing,
And armed trust which never can despair,
And hopes good hap how ever fatal dear.
Idem. The man that has of Fortitude and might,
And thereto has a Kingdom void withal,
Except he also guide himself aright,
His power and strength prevails but a small,
He cannot escape at length, an hapless fall.
I. H. M. of M. The Roman Sergius having lost his hand,
Slew with one hand four in a single fight,
A thing all reason ever did withstand,
But that bright Fortitude spreads forth her light.
Pompey by shore held from The Italian Land,
And all his saylors quaking in his sight,
First hoysed sails, and cried amidst the strife,
There Is need I go, no need to save my life.
I. Markeham. Force without wisdom, is of little worth.
G. Gascoigne. Greater force there needs to maintain wrong the^ right.
Ed. Spencer. Agis that guilt the Lacedemon street,
Entending one day battle with his foes,
By counsel was repeld as thing unmeet,
The enemy being ten to one in shoes,
But he replied, it is needful that his feet
With many heads, should lead to many blows.
And one being good, an army is for ten:
Foes to Religion, and known naughty men,
To him that told Dineceus how his foes
Covered the sun with darts and armed spears.
He made reply, thy news is joy in woes,
We Will in the shadow fight, and conquer foes.
I. Markham. -- As to love, the life for virtues flame,
Is the just act of a true noble will:
So to contemn it, and her hopes exclude
Is baseness, rashness, and no Fortitude.
Idem. Rash Isadas the Lacedemon Lord,
That naked fought against the Theban power,
Although they chained his valor by a cord,
Yet was he find for rashness in that hour.
And those which most his careless praise afford,
Did most condemn what folly did devour:
For in attempting, prowess is not meant,
But wisely doing what we do attempt.
Idem. Felicity.
O had Felicity feeling of woe?
Or could on mean but moderately feed?
Or would look down the way that he must go?
Or could abstain from what diseases breed?
To stop the wound before to death it bleed?
War should not fill Kings palaces with moan,
Nor peril come, when it is least thought upon.
M. Drayton. Folly Fools.
Folly in youth is sin, in age is madness.
S. Daniel. A greater sign of Folly is not shown,
Then trusting others force, distrust our own:
S. I. Harr. Transl. -- Wicked men repine their sins to hear,
And Folly flings, if counsel touch him near.
D. Lodge. Fair fools delight to be accounted wise.
Chapter Marlowe. Fools will find fault without the cause discerning.
And argue most of that they have no learning.
S. I. Harr. Transl. -- There is a method, time, and place,
Which fools observing do co^mence, ere wise me^ have their grace.
W. Warner. It Is better be a fool then be a fox,
For Folly is rewarded and respected,
Where subtlety is hated and rejected.
D. Lodge* -- The foolish commmons use
Obey them most, who does them most abuse.
S. I. Harrington. P. A witless fool may every man him guess,
That leaves the more, and takes him to the less.
G. Gascoigne. Flatterer.
-- Foul leasings and vile flattery,
Two filthy blots in noble genterie.
Ed. Sp. When as we finely soothe our own desires,
Our best conceits do prove the greatest hers.
M. Drayton. Nere was pretense so foul, but some would flatter it*
Nor any thing so pestilent, as misapplied wit.
W. Warner. To be officious, gets friends, plain dealing hated is
Yet better plainly to reprove, then fainedly to kis:
We cannot also love our friends, and Flatter their amiss.
Idem. -- Flattery can never want rewards.
D. Lodge. He twice offends, who sin in flattery bears,
Yet every hour he dies, who ever fears.
D. Lodge. The Lords and Ladies over rent, and cunningly the fine,
The Parasite does ouerreach, and bears away the gain.
W. Warner. Ye sons of craft bearing as many faces,
As Proteus, takes among the marine places,
And force your natures all the best you can,
To counterfeit the grace of some great man
Chamelion like, who takes him in each hew,
Of black or white, or yellow, green, or blew,
That comes him next, so you that find the fashion
To hurt the poor, with many a great taxation,
You that do prease to have the princes ear,
To make your names in provinces appear,
Ye subtle Thurins sell your fumish wind
To wicked wights, whose senses ye do blind.
Th. Hudson. Transl. Time fawning spaniels, Mermaids on the earth.
Trencher fed flies.
Base Parasites, these elbowe-rubbing mates,
A plague to all lascivious wanton states:
O filthy monkies, vile and beastly kind,
Foul prating Parrats, birds of Harpy brood,
A corasiue to every noble mind.
Vipers that suck your mothers dearest blood:
Misshapen monster, worst of any creature,
A foe to all, an enemy to nature.
M. Drayton. Fortune.
Fortune as blind as he whom she does lead,
Her feature chaung'd each minute of the hour,
Her riggish feet fantastickly would tread:
Now would she smile, and suddenly would lowre,
And with one breath, her words are sweet and sour.
Upon her foes she amorously does glaunce,
And on her followers coyly look askaunce,
About her neck (it seemed as for a chain)
Some Princes crowns and broken scepters hung.
Upon her arm a lazy youth did lean,
Which scornfully unto the ground she flung,
And with a wanton grace passing alone,
Great bags of gold from out her bosom drew,
And to base Pesants and fond Idiots threw.
A duskie vale which hid her sightlesse-eies
Like clouds, which cover our uncertain lives,
Painted about with bloody Tragedies,
Fools wearing crowns and wise men clogged in given
Now how she gives again, how she depriues:
In this black map this she her might discouers,
In Camps and Courts, on soldiers, and on lovers.
M. Drayton. A hap, a chance, a casual event,
The vulgars Idol, and a childish terror:
A what man will, a silly accident
The mask of blindness, and disguise of error,
Natures vile nickname, follies foolish mirror:
A term, a byword, by tradition learned,
A hearsay, nothing not to be discernd,
A wanton fear, a silly Infants dream,
A vain illusion, a mere fantasy:
A seeming shade, a lunaticke man's dream.
A fond Aenigma, a flat heresy.
Imaginations doting emperie.
A folly in it self, it one self loathing,
A thing that would be, and yet can be nothing.
Disease of time, ambitions concubine:
A mind intrancing snare, a slippery ice,
The bait of death, destructions heady wine.
Vaine-glories patron, the fools paradise.
Fond hope wherewith confusion does entice.
A vile seducing f^end, which haunts men still,
To loose them in the errors of their will.
Idem. O fortune the great Amorite of kings,
Opinions breath, thou Epicurian air:
Invention of man's soul, falsest of things,
A step beyond our judgment, and a staire
Higher then men can reach with reasons wings.
Thou blindfold Archeresse, thou that wilt not hear:
Thou foe to persons, manners, times and all,
That raisest worthless, while the worthiest fall.
I. Markham. Ah fortune, nurse of fools, poison of hope,
Fuel of vain desires, deserts destruction*
Supposed sovereign, through our vain construction
Princes of Paganism, root of impie^ie,
Devil on earth, masked in piety.
Scorn of the learned, follies elder scholar,
Bastard of time, begot by vain opinion:
Against thy power, a peevish proud resister.
Mother of lies, and witness of illusion:
Lamp of vainglory, double faced shroe,
Who smiles at first, succesfull, ends in woe.
D. Lodge. Who wins her grace, must with atchiuements woe he^
As she is blind, so never had she ears,
Nor must with puling eloquence go to her:
She understands not sighs, she hears not prayers.
Flattered she flies; controld she ever fears.
And though a while she nicely do forsake it,
She i^ a woman, and at length will take it.
Nor ever let him dream once of a crown,
For one bad cast that will give up his game,
And though by ill hap he be overthrown,
Yet let him manage her till she be tame.
M. Drayton. Fortune the folly is, and plague of those
Which to the world their wretched will dispose.
M. of M. All flesh is frail and full of fickleness,
Subject to fortunes charm, still changing new,
What haps to day to me, to morrow may to you.
Ed. Spencer. Fortune the foe to famous chieuisance,
Sildome or never yields to virtue aid:
But in her way throws mischief and mischance,
Whereby her course is stopped, and passage laid.
Idem. Mock Gods they are, and many Gods induce,
Who fortune feign to father there abuse.
M. of M. -- In vain do men
The heavens of there fortunes fault accuse,
Sith they know best what is the best for them,
For they to each such fortune do diffuse,
As they do know each can most aptly use:
For not that which men covet most is best,
Nor that thing worst which men do most refuse.
But fittest is, that all contented rest
With that they hold: each has his fortune in his breast.
Ed. Spencer. No fortune is so bad, our selves ne frame
There is no chance at all has us preserved.
There is no fate whom we have need to blame:
There is no desteny but is deserved:
No luck that leaves us safe, or vnpreseru'd.
Let us not then complain of fortunes skill,
For all our good descends from Gods good will,
And of our lewdness, springs all our ill.
M. of M. -- They that do dwell on fortunes call,
No sooner rise, but ready are to fall.
D. Lodge. Look how much higher fortune does erect
The climing wight on her unstable wheel:
So much the nigher may a man exspect
To see his head where late he saw his heel.
Policrates has proved it in effect,
And Dyonisius that too true did feel
Who long were luld on hie in fortunes lap:
And fell down suddenly to great mishap.
On the other side, the more man is oppressed
And utterly overthrown by fortunes lowre,
The sooner comes his state to be redressed,
When wheel shall turn and bring the happy hour.
Some from the Block have grown to be so blessed.
Whole realms have been subverted to their power.
As Marius and Ventidius sample is,
In former age, and Jews of France in this.
S. I. Harr. Transl. -- As the boystrous wind
Does shake the tops of highest reared towers,
So does the force of froward fortune strike
The wight that highest sits in haughty state.
G. Gascoigne. -- So wills the wanton queen of chance,
That each man trace this Labyrinth of life:
With slippery steps now wronged by fortune strange,
Now drawn by counsel from the maze of strife.
D. Lodge. We all are proud when fortune favors us,
As if inconstant chance were always one:
Or standing now, she would continue thus,
O fools look back, and see the rolling stone
Whereon she blindly lighting sets her foot,
And slightly sowes, that sildome takes root.
Th. Kyd. Fortune the first and last that gouernes states.
I. Markham. The blindfold mistress of uncertain change.
D. Lodge. The wayward lady of this wicked world.
Idem. Blind fortune fails mighty ones, and meaner does advance.
W. Warner. Blind fortune finds none so fit to flout
As Sures by sotts, which cast no kind of doubt.
M. of M. -- Fortune cannot raise
Any one aloft without some others wrack,
Floods drown no fields unless they find a bracke.
Idem. Where power dwelles and riches rest,
False fortune is a comely guest.
E. of S. Think fortune newly hatched is fledge, and waggeth wing to fly
All suffer change, our selves new born, even then begin to die.
VV. Warner. The man that fortune at command will keep,
He must be sure he never let her sleep.
M. Drayton. There never yet was Emperor or King,
Could boast that he had fortune in a string.
S. I. Harr. Transl. -- All things to fortune are subjected,
Chiefly in wars, that are by chance directed.
Idem. Wheresoever fortune her bounty will bestow,
There heaven and earth must pay what she does owe.
M. of M. The man whose thoughts to fortunes height aspires,
Were better die then live in low desires.
Th. Achelly. Admit thou had Pactolian waves to land thee gold at will,
Know Craesus did to Cyrus kneel, and thou may speed as ill.
W. Warner. Attempt not things beyond thy reach, join fortune to thy will,
Least Phoebus chair do else surcharge rash Phaethon his skill
Idem. If fortune help who^ thou woldst hurt, fret not at it the more,
When Ajax stormed, then from him the prize Ulysses bore.
Idem. Good fortune draws from heaven her descent,
Making hie Jove the root of her large tree:
She shows from him how many Godheads went,
Archangels, Angels, heavens posterity,
From thence she shows the glorious third she lent,
To Monarkes, Emperors and Kings in fee.
Annexing as collateralls to her love,
Honor, virtue, valor and endless time.
N^thelesse ill fortune will be elder borne
She says she springs from Saturn, Jove's wronged sire,
And heaven and earth, and hell, her coat have borne
Fresh bleeding hearts within a field of fire:
All that the world admires she makes her scorn,
Who farthest seems, is to ill fortune near.
And that just proof may her great praise commend,
All that good chance begins, ill chance does end.
I. Markham. Ill fortune is attended by reproach,
Good fortune fame and virtue stellifies.
Idem. -- What man can shun the hap,
That hidden lies, unawares him to surprise:
Misfortune waits advantage to entrappe
The man most wary, in her whelming lap.
Ed. Spencer. The fortune that misfortune does afford,
Is for to live and die unfortunate.
Th. Achelly. Misfortune follows him that tempts fortune.
Chapter Fitz Ieffrey. Friendship. Friends.
The natural affection soon does cease,
And quenched is with Cupids greater flame:
But faithful friendship does them both suppress:
And them with maistring discipline does tame
Through thoughts aspiring, to eternal fame.
Ed. Spencer. In friendship, sovereign it is as Mithridate,
Thy friend to love, as one whom thou may hate.
M. of M. Ne certes can that friendship long endure,
How ever gay and goodly be the style,
That does ill cause or evil end endure,
For virtue is the band which binds hearts most sure.
Ed. Spencer. -- Enmity that of no ill proceeds,
But of occasion, with the occasion ends,
And friendship which a faint affection breeds,
Without regard of good, lies like ill grounded seed.
Ed. Spencer. With virtue choose thy friend, with virtue him retain,
Let virtue be the ground, so shall it not be vain.
S. Th. Wiat. Try friends by touch, a feeble fre^d may prove thy stro^gest foe*
Great Pompeys head to Caesar's hand, it was betrayed so,
VV. Warner. In perfect friendship no suspect, for two in one are all,
Community, or doubling joy, or making grief more small.
Idem. The truest friendship in misery is tried,
For then will none but faithful friends abide,
G. Turberuile. Right true it is, and said full yore ago,
Take heed of him that by the back thee claweth,
For none is worse, then is the friendly foe,
Though thee seem good, all things yt thee delights:
Yet know it well, that in thy bosom creeps,
For many a man such fires often times he kindles,
That with the blaze his beard himself he sings.
E. of Surrey. None can deem right who faithful friends do rest,
While they bear sway and rule in hie degree:
For then both fast and fained friends are pressed.
Whose faiths seem both of one effect to be.
But then reuolts the first and fained guest,
When wealth vnwindes and fortune seems to fly,
But he that loves indeed, remains fast,
And loves and serves when life and all is past.
S. I. Harr. Transl. Often times we see in house of mean estate,
In fortunes bad, and chances overthwart:
That men do sooner lay away debate,
And join in sound accord with hand and heart,
Then Princes courts, where riches genders hate:
And vile suspect that loving minds does part.
Where charity is clean consumde and vanished,
And friendship firm, is quite cast out and banished.
Idem. Who so wants friends to back what he begins,
In lands far off, gets not, although he wins.
S. Daniell. If fortune friendly fawn, and lend thee wealthy store,
Thy friends conioined joy, does make thy joy the more
If frowardly she frown, and drives to distress:
His aid releeues thy ruth, and makes thy solace less.
S. Th. Wiat. They are not always surest friends on who^ we most do spend.
W. Warner. True friends have feeling of each others woe,
And when ones heart is sad, all theirs is so.
Chapter Middleion. A golden treasure is the ^ried friend,
But who may gold from counterfeits defend?
Trust not to soon, nor yet to soon mistrust,
With the one thy self, with the other thy friend thou hurtst,
Who twines betwixt, and stears the golden mean,
Nor rashly loves, nor mistrusts in vain.
Mir. of M. -- Friends are geason now a days,
And grow to fume before they taste the fire:
Aquersitie bereauing man's auailes,
They fly like feathers dallying in the wind.
They rise like bubbles in a stormy rain,
Swelling in words, and flying faith and deeds.
D. Lodge. Faint friends when they fall out, most cruel foemen be.
Ed. Spencer. Better a new friend, then an old foe is said.
Idem. Frugalitie.
He that will thrive, must think no courses vile.
B. Johnson. No hurt but good (who means to multiply)
Bought wit is dear, and dressed with sower sauce,
Repentance comes too late, and then say I,
Who spares the first, and keeps the last unspent*
Shall find that sparing yields a goodly rent.
G. Gascoigne. Let first thine own hand hold fast all that comes,
But let the other learn his letting fly:
Idem. Fury.
-- Fury furiously man's life assailes
With thousand cannons, sooner felt then seen,
Where weakest, strongest, fraught with deadly teene,
Blind, crooked, blisterd, melancholy, sad,
Many-nam'd poison, minister of death,
Which from us creepes, but to us gallopeth.
Foul, trouble rest, phantasticke, greedy-gut,
Blood sweating, hearts-theefe, wretched, filthy-slut
The child of surfait and aires-temper vicious,
Perilous known, but unknown most pernicious.
I. Syluister. -- Fury cruel cursed wight,
That unto Knighthood works much shame and woe,
And that same hag, his aged mother hight,
Occasion, the root of all wrath and dispight.
With her, who so will raging Fury tame,
Must first begin, and welther amenage,
First her restrain from her reproachful blame
And evil means, with which she does enrage
Her frantic son, and kindles his courage,
Then when she is withdrawn, or strong withstood,
Is eath his Idle Fury to assuage,
And calm this tempest of his passion wood,
The banks are overflowed, when so sped is the flood.
Ed. Spencer. Fury was red with rage, his eyes did glowe,
While flakes of fire from forth his mouth did flow
His hands and arms y bathed in blood of those
Whom fortune, sin, or fate made countries foes.
T. Lodge. -- This fell fury, for forerunner sends
Many and phrenzie, to subborne her friends,
Whereof the one drying, the other ouerwarming.
The feeble brain (the edge of judgment harming)
Within the soul phantastickly they feign,
A confused host of strange Chimeraes vain.
I. Syluister. Gifts.
It Is wisdom to give much, a Gift preuailes,
When deep persuading Oratorie fails.
Chapter Marlowe. A giving hand though foul, shall have fair praise.
S. Daniell. -- The greatest Gifts whereof we boast,
Are those which do attempt and tire us most.
T. Lodge. -- Only wisdom grave, and judgments clear,
Gifts given from heaven, that are not common here.
S. I. H. Transl. Goods Gifts are often given to men past good.
G. Chapman. Good Gifts abused, to man's confusion turn.
Th. Dekkar. Testators and Executors so give and so receive,
As doubtful whethers joy or grief, is more to take or leave
For as do hogs their troghs to hou^ds, so these give and get place
Death not the Dier gives bequests, and therefore but grave grace.
W. Warner. To loiter well deserved Gifts, is not to give but sell,
When to requite ingratitude, were to do evil well.
Idem. Gentleness.
The gentle mind by gentle deeds is known,
For a man by nothing is so well bewraide,
As by his manners, in which plain is shown,
Of what degree, and what race he is grown.
Ed. Spencer. Sweet gentleness is Bewties waiting maid.
Th. Watson. -- In gentle thoughts,
Relenting thoughts, remorse, and pity rests.
Chapter Marlowe. All like as Phoebus, with his cheerful beams,
Does freshly force the fragrant flowers to flourish,
So gentle rulers subjects love do nourish.
I. H. Mir. of M. Like as the gentle heart it self bewraies,
In doing gentle deeds with francke delight:
Even so the baser mind it self displaies,
In canckered malice, and revenge for spite.
W. Shakespeare. O what an easy thing is it to discrie
The gentle blood, how ever it be wrapped,
In sad misfortunes foul deformity
And wretched sorrows which have often hapt.
For howsoever it may grow mishapt,
That to all virtue it may seem unapt,
Yet will it show some sparks of gentle mind,
And at the last, break forth in his own proper kind.
Ed. Spencer. Gentry.
-- True Gentry stands in the trade
Of virtuous life, not in the fleshly line,
For blood is knit, but Gentry is divine.
I. H. M. of M. Above cognizance or arms, or pedigree far,
An unspotted coat, is like a blazing star.
G. Gascoigne. Kind Amalthea was transformd by Jove,
Into his sparkling pauement, for his love,
Though but a goat, and giving him her milk,
Bazenes is flinty Gentry, soft as silk.
In heaven she lives, and rules a living sign
In humane bodies: yet not so divine,
That she can work her kindness in our hearts.
G. Chapman. The true Gentilitie by their own arms
Advance themselves, the falls by others harms.
Th. Bastard. Gluttony.
-- By his side rode loathsome Gluttony,
Deformed creature, on a filthy swine:
His belly was vpblowen with luxury,
And eke with fatnes, swollen were his eine.
And like a Crane, his neck was long and fine,
With which he swallowed up excessive feast,
For want of which, poor people often did pine,
And all the way most like a brutish swine,
He spued up his gorge, that all did him detest.
Ed. Spencer. Fat paunches have lean pates, and dainty bits
Make rich the ribs, but bankrout quite the wits.
W. Shakespeare. Your appetites O gluttons to content,
The sacred breast of Thetis blew, is rent:
The air must be dispeopled for your mawes,
The Phoenix sole can scarce escape your claws.
Th. Hudson. Transl. Of little nature lives, superfluous meat
But dulls the spirit, and does the stomach freate.
Idem. Who fares finest, does but feed, and ouerfeedeth often,
Who sleeps softest does but sleep, and sometimes ouersoft.
VV. warner. -- Excess does work access to sin.
Idem. O plague, O poison to the warlike state,
Thou make the noble hearts effeminate,
While Rome was ruled by Curioes and Fabrices,
