And grossly though a schism, yet has each Schismatic his brother
Mean while the learned want their meed, and none with profit hears,
The tedious dolt whose artlesse tongue does preach to very ears.
VV. Warner. -- Since pure religion does install
Learned professors, Prelates of deserts,
Let them aspire and reac instructed hearts
Against the base bestowers of church livings,
That use their graunts in tellings, not in giuings.
Th. Storer. Repentance.
Repentance makes two rivers of her eyes,
Her humble face dares scant behold the skies:
Her broken breast is beaten blew and black,
Her tender fleshis rent wih rugged sack,
With sorrows snowes her hoary waxen head,
With ashes pale, and dust is ouerspread.
I. Syluister. Repentance, hope, and soft humility,
Do flank the wings of faiths triumphant carre,
Idem. Repentance,
A salve, a comfort, and a cordial,
He that has her, the keys of heaven has,
This is the guide, this is the port, the path.
M. Drayton. O happy they that keep within their measure,
To turn their course in time, and sound retreit,
Before that wit which late Repentance tought,
Were better never had then so dear bought.
S. I. H. Sins have their salues, repentance can do much.
R. Green. -- To be penitent for faults, with it a paron bears.
W. W. Then hope we health when sin is left repentantly in heart,
Add then new life, and we to God, God does to us conuart.
Idem. Yet stay thy feet in murders ugly gate,
Ill comes to soon, repentance often too late.
M. Dr, Their lives no man so settled in content,
That has not daily whereof to repent.
D. Lodge. We see what is good, and thereto we consent,
But yet we choose the worse and soon repent.
S. Daniell. Rest.
-- What so strong,
But wanting rest, will also want of might?
The sun that measures heaven all day long,
At night does bath his steeds, The Ocean waves among.
Ed. Spencer. Untroubled night they say, gives counsel best.
Idem. Who long has rested cannot run apace,
The fettered horse is hindmost in the chase.
Revenge.
-- Next within the entry of the gate,
Sat fell revenge, gnashing her teeth with ire,
Devising means how she may vengeance take,
Never in rest till she have her desire.
But frets within so far forth with the fire
Of wreaking flames, that now determines she,
To die by death, or vengd by death to be.
M. Sackuill. O fearful frowning Nemesis,
Daughter of justice most severe,
That art the worlds great arbitresse,
And Queen of causes reigning here.
S. Daniell. Fierce Nemesis mother of fate and change,
Sword bearer of the eternal providence.
Idem. -- Nemesis whose hasty revenging
Hands are ever at hand: whose mind is mutable always,
At miseries laughing, at men's felicity grudging.
A. France. -- Nemesis hie mistress of revenge,
That with the scourge keeps all the world in awe.
Th. Dekkar. The mind by wrong is made a malcontent,
And clouds her shine in pleaslesse melancholy,
Her holy humors are in passion spent,
Till by revenge she is set at liberty.
For it is revenge that satisfaction brings
To injured minds, and to oppressed things.
I. Markham. The soul is like a boystrous working sea,
Swelling in billows for disdain of wrongs,
And tumbling up and down from bay to bay,
Proves great with child of indignations.
Yet with revenge is brought to calm allay,
Disburdend of the pain thereto belongs.
Her bowers are turned to bright-fac't sunshine braues,
And fair content plays gently on her waves.
Idem. Revenge dies not, rigor begets new wrath,
And blood has never glory, mercy has.
S. D. Revenge is mine, says he that sits on hie.
Th. Achelly. O dire revenge when thou in time art rakte,
From out the ashes that preserve thee long,
And lightly from thy cinders art awakte,
Fuel to freedom, and revived with wrong:
How soon from sparks the greatest flames art sprung?
Which does by nature to his top aspire,
Whose massy greatness once kept down his fire.
M. Drayton. Revenge in tears does ever wash his hands,
Idem. Who so does threat means of revenge does loose.
S. D. Had I revenged been of every harm,
My coat had never kept me half so warm.
G. Gascoigne. Though vengeance come behind, and her foot sore,
She ouertakes the offender going before.
Riches. Description of Mammon.
At last he came unto a gloomy glade,
Covered with boughs and shades from heaven light:
Whereas he sitting found in secret shade,
An uncouth, saluage, and uncivil wight,
Of grisly hue, and foul ill-favored sight:
His face with smoke was tand, and eyes were bleard,
His head and beard with sowte were all bedight,
His coal black hands did seem to have been seard,
In smith-fiers spitting forge, and nails like claws appeard.
His Iron coat all ouergrowne with rust,
Was underneath enueloped with gold,
Whose glittering gloss darkened with filthy dust
Well it appeared to have been of old,
A work of rich entraile and curious molde,
Wouen with Anticks and wild Imagerie,
And in his lap a mass of coin he told
And turned vpside down to feed his eye,
A covetous desire with his huge treasury:
And round about him lay on every side,
Great heaps of gold that never could be spent,
Of Mulcibers devouring element:
Some others were nere driven and distent
Into great Ingoes and to wedges square,
Some in round plates without monument:
But some were stamped, and in their end all bare,
The Anticke shapes of Kings and Keysars, strange and rare.
Ed. Spencer. Mammon. -- I riches read
And deem them root of all disquietnes:
First got with guile, and then preserved with dread,
And after spent with pride and lauishnes:
Leaving behind them grief and heaviness.
Infinite mischiefs of them do arise,
Strife and debate, blood-shead and bitterness,
Outrageous wrong, and hellish couetize,
That noble heart as great dishonor does despise.
Idem. -- It Is but a little slide
That does the house of riches from her mouth divide.
Before the door sat selfe-consuming care,
Day and night keeping wary watch and ward:
For fear least fotce or fraud should unaware
Break in and spoil the treasure there ingard.
Ne would he suffer sleep once thitherward
Approach, albe his drowsy den were next,
For next to death is sleep to be compared,
Therefore his house is unto his annexed,
Here sleep, there riches, and hell gate them both betwixt.
Ed. Spencer. Well may a rich man's hearse want tears, but heirs he shall not
miss,
To whom that he is dead at length no little joy it is.
vv. warner. Good is no good, but if it be spend,
God gives good for no other end.
Ed. Spencer. Vessels of brass, often handled brightly shine,
What difference between the richest mine
And basest earth, but use? for both not used
Are of little worth: then treasure is abusde
When misers keep it, being put to lone,
In time it will return us two for one.
Chapter Marlowe. Gold is a suitor, never took repulse,
It carries Palm with it, (where e're it goes)
Respect, and observation; it vncouers
The knottie heads of the most surly Grooms,
Enforcing iron doors to yield it way,
Were they as strong ram'd up as Aetna gates.
It bends the hams of Gossip Vigilance,
And makes her supple feet, as swift as wind.
It thawes the frostiest, and most stiff disdain:
Muffles the clearness of Election,
Strains fancy unto foul Apostacy.
And strikes the quickest-sighted Judgment blind.
Then why should we despair? despair? Away:
Where Gold's the Motive, women have no Nay.
B. Johnson. Wealth in this age will scarcely look on merit.
Idem. -- Gentry does small avail,
And virtue less, if lands and riches fail.
S. I. H. Sacrilege.
The common text shall have a common gloss,
Receipts in parcels, shall be paid in gross.
This doctrine preached who from the church does take
At last shall trebble restitution make.
M. Dray. Secrecy.
-- Secrecy the crown of a true Lover.
M. Drayton. -- Hard it is to prove
By sight or speech, what bides in secret breast.
S. I. H. -- What can so secret be,
But out of it will when we do least suspect?
For posts have ears, and walls have eyes to see,
Dumb beasts and birds have toongs ill to detect.
Idem. Silence.
Dumb Silence, sworn attendant on black night,
Thou that have power to close up murmures iawe:
To stop the barking of the watchful hound,
And charm the gagling of those waking foul,
That saved Jove's Capitol, mild Queen of rest.
Th. Dekkar. Soft Silence, and submisse obedience,
Both linkt together never do depart:
Both gifts of God, nor gotten but from thence,
Both girlonds of his saints, against their foes offense.
Ed. Spencer. -- Silence wisdoms mother.
S. Phil. Sidney. Silence does seem the mask of base oppression.
Idem. Senses.
Although things sensible be numberless,
But only five the Senses organs be:
And in those five all things their forms express,
Which we can touch, taste, feel, or hear or see.
I. Dauies. Man's eye makes what is seen to seem so fair,
Man's ear makes what is heard to sound so sweet:
His touch by softness every sense is meet
For his own object.
Idem. Sight.
-- The two eyes which have the seeing power,
Stand as one watchman, spy, or Sentinel:
Being placed aloft within the heads hie tower,
And though both seeing, yet both but one thing tell.
Idem. -- Nine things to sight required are,
The power to see, the light, the visible thing:
Being not too small, too thin, too nigh, too far,
Clear space; and time the form distinct to bring.
Idem. Like as a glass is an inanimate eye,
And outward forms imbraceth outwardly,
So is the eye an amimate glass that shows
In forms without us.
G. Chapman. What we behold is censured by the eyes,
Where both deliberate the love is slight:
Who ever loved, that loved not at first sight?
Chapter Marlowe. I trow that countenance cannot lie,
Whose thoughts are legible in the eye.
M. Roydon. Often the eye mistakes, the brain being troubled.
W. Sha. All amorous eyes observing form, thinks parts obscured best.
vv. warner. A greedy eye will have a greedy hand.
D. Lodge. -- A monstrous rabblement
Of foul misshapen wights, of which some were
Headed like Owls, with beakes uncomely bent:
Others like dogs, others like gryphons dreare,
And some had wings, and some had claws to tear.
And every one of them had Linceus eyes,
And every one did bows and arrows bear.
All those were lawless lusts, corrupt envy,
And covetous aspects, all cruel enemies.
Those same against the bulwark of the sight
Did lay strange siege and battailous assault,
Ne once did yield it respit day or night,
But soon as Titan began his head exault,
And soon again as he his light withhault
Their wicked engines they against it bent:
That is each thing by which the eyes may fault.
But to them all more huge and violent,
Beauty and money, they that bulwark shroudly rent.
Ed. Spencer. Hearing.
Ears office is the troubled air to take,
Which in their mazes forms a sound or noise,
Whereof her self does true distinction make.
The wickets of the soul are placed on hie,
Because all sounds do lightly mount aloft:
And that they may not pierce too violently,
They are delaid with turns and windings often.
I. Dauies. As streams which with their winding banks do play,
Stopped by their creekes run softly through the plain:
So in the ears labyrinth the voice does stay,
And does with easy notice touch the brain.
Idem. It is the slow'st yet the daintiest sense,
For even the ears of such as have no skill,
Perceive a discord and conceive offense,
And knowing not what is good, yet find the ill.
Idem. These conduit pipes of knowledge the mind,
But the other three attend the body still:
For by their services the soul does find
What things are to the body good or ill.
I. Dauies. The second bulwark was the hearing sense,
Against which the second troop designment makes
Deformed creatures, in strange difference,
Some having heads like hearts, some like to snakes,
Some wild like boares, late rowz'd out of the brakes.
Slanderous reproaches and foul infamies,
Leasings, backbitings, and vainglorious crake.
Bad counsels, praises and false flatteries,
All those against that first did send their batteries.
Ed. Spencer. Smelling.
Next, in the nosthrils she does use the Smell,
As God the breath of life in them did give:
So makes he now his power in them to dwell,
To judge all aires whereby we breathe and hue.
This sense is also mistress of an art,
Which to soft people sweet petfumes does sell:
Through this dear art does little good impart,
Since they smell best that does of nothing smell.
And ye good sents do purify the brain,
Awake the fancy, and the wits refine:
Hence old devotion in advise did ordain,
To make men's spirits more apt to thoughts divine.
I. Dauies. Likewise that same third fort that is the smell,
Of that third troop was cruelly assaide:
Whose hideous shapes were like to fiends of hell.
Some like to hounds, some like to apes dismaide.
Some like to puttocks all in plumes arraide,
All shapte according their conditions,
For by those ougly forms werren portraide
Foolish delights and fond abusions,
Which do that sense besiege with light illusio^s.
Ed. Sp. Tasting
The bodies life with meats and air is fed,
Therefore the soul does use the tasting power,
In veins which through the tongue and pallat spread.
Distinguish every relish sweet and sower.
This is the bodies nurse: but since man's wit
Found the Art of cookery to delight his sense,
More bodies are consumde and killed with it,
Then with the sword, famine, or pestilence.
I. Dauies. -- That fourth band which cruel battery bent
Against the fourth bulwark, that is the taste:
Was as the rest, a grisly rabblement,
Some mouth like greedy Estriges, some fac'st
Like loathly Toades, some fashioned in the waste
Like swine, for so deserved his luxury,
Surfeit, misdiet, and unthrifty warke,
Vain feasts, and idle superfluity,
All those this senses fort assail incessantly.
Ed. Sp. Feeling.
Lastly, the feeling power which is life's root,
Through every living part it self does shed,
By sinews which extend from head to foot,
And like a net all over the body spread.
Much like a subtle spider which does sit
In middle of her web which spreddeth wide:
If ought do touth the outmost thread of it,
She feels it instantly on every side.
I. Dauies. By touch the first pure qualities we learn,
Which quicken all things, hot, cold, moist, and dry:
By touch, hard, soft, rough, swoot, we do discern,
By touch, sweet pleasure and sharp pain we try.
These are the outward instruments of sense.
These are the guardes which every one must pass,
Ere it approach the minds intelligence,
Or touch the fantasy, wits looking glass.
Idem. But the fifth troop most horrible of hue,
And fierce of force was dreadful to report:
For some like snailes, some did like spiders show,
And some like ougly vrchins thick and short,
Cruelly they assailed that fifth fort*
Armed with darts of sensual delight,
With strings of carnal lust and strong effect.
Of feeling pleasures, with which day and night
Against the same fifth bulwark they continued fight.
Ed. Spencer. Sin.
First we do taste the fruit, then see our sin.
S. Daniell. Shame follows sin, disgrace is daily given,
Impiety will out, never so closely done,
No walls can hide us from the eye of heaven,
For shame must end what wickedness begun,
Forth breaks reproach when we least think thereon.
Idem. Like as diseases common cause of death,
Bring danger most when least they prick and smart:
Which is a sign they have expulst the breath
Of lively heat which does defend the heart,
Even so such sins as felt are on no part,
Have conquered grace, and by their wicked ure,
So killed the soul that it can have no cure.
I. Hig. M. of M. Sins harvest never fails, but grace has death.
D. Lodge. Cover thou fire never so close within,
Yet out it will, and so will secret sin.
M. of M. It doubles sin if finely sin we practice to prevent.
W.W. Man may securely sin, but safely never.
B. Ihonson. What wight on earth can void of fault be found?
What Saint is that who does not sin sometime?
Tweene good and bad this difference sole is found,
That good men sin but seld, and mend betime.
The bad man (making scruple none nor question)
Yields willingly to every leaud suggestion.
S. I. H. Sins often assaid, ere thought to be no sin,
So soileth sin, the soul it sinketh in.
M. of M. Shame leaves us by degrees, not at first coming,
For nature checks a new offense with loathing.
But use of sin does make it seem as nothing.
S. Daniell. What though our sins go brave and better clad?
They are as those in rags, as base, as bad.
Idem. The spot is foul, though by a Monarch made,
Kings cannot privilege a sin forbade.
Idem. -- Sin ever must
Be torturde with the rack of his own frame,
For he that holds no faith, shall find no trust,
But sowing wrong, is sure to reap the same.
Idem. -- Cunning sin being clad in virtues shape,
Flies much reproof, and many storms does escape.
D. Lodge. -- Place for people, people place, and all for sin decay.
vv. warner. To punish sin is good, it is no nay,
They wrecke not sin, but merit wrecke for sin
The fathers fault that wreake upon the kin.
M. of M. The sin to which a man by love is driven,
So much the rather ought to be forgiven.
S. I. H. Slander.
Her face was ugly, and her mouth distort,
Foming with poison round about her gils,
In which her cursed tongue full sharp and short,
Appeard like Aspes sting, that closely kills,
Or cruelly does wound, whom so she wills,
A distaffe in her other hand she had,
Upon the which she little spins but spils,
And feign to weaue false tales and leasings bad
To throw amongst the gods which others had dispred.
Ed. Sp. Her nature is, all goodness to abuse,
And causeless crimes continually to frame:
With which she guiltless persons may abuse,
And stole away the crown of her good name,
Ne ever knight so bold, ne ever dame
So chaste and loyal lived, but she would strive
With forged cause, them falsely to defame.
Ne ever thing was done so well alive,
But she with blame would blot, and of due praise deprive.
Idem. All like the stings of Asps, that kill with smart,
Her spightfull words do pierce and wound the inner part.
Idem. Foul canker of fair virtuous action,
Vile blaster of the fresh bloomes here on earth,
Enuies abhorred child detraction.
I. Marston. Happy is he that lives in such a sort,
That need not fear the tongues of false report.
E. of S. The vulgar tongues are armed evermore
With slanderous brute, to blemish the renown
Of virtuous dames; which though at first it spring,
Of slender cause, yet does it swell so fast,
As in short space it fills every ear
With swift report of undeserved blame.
G. Gascoigne. -- It ever has been known,
They other virtues scorn that doubt their own.
S. Daniell. No plaster heales a deadly poisoned sore,
No secret hid where slander keeps the door.
M. Drayton. Against bad tongues goodness cannot defend her,
Those be most free from faults, they least will spare,
But prate of them whom they have scantly known,
Judging their humors to be like their own.
S. I. H. Slander once set on foot though false, is talked in every street.
VV. Warner. No wound with warlike hand of enemy
Inflict with dint of sword so sore does light,
As does the poisonous sting which infamy
Infuseth in the name of noble wight.
It never can recured be again,
Ne all the skill which that immortal spright
Of Podalyrius did in it retain,
Can remedy such hurts: such hurts are hellish pain.
Ed. Sp. A sprightly wit disdaines detraction.
I. Marston. Backbiting pens, and pens that sooth up sin,
^nuious the one, the other clawbacks binne.
I. Syl. Sleep.
Amidst a dark thick wood there is a cave,
Whose entrance is with Iuie ouerspread,
They have no light within, nor none they crave,
^ere Sleep does couch her ouerdrowsie head,
and sloth lies by that seems the gout to have.
and Idleness not so well taught as fed,
^hey point forgetfulness the gate to keep,
that none come out or in to hinder Sleep.
^he knows no means of men, ne none will learn,
their messages she list not understand:
She knows no business does her concern,
Silence is Sentinel of all this band,
And unto those he coming does discern
To come too near, he beckens with his hand,
He treads soft, his shoes are made of felt,
His garment short, and girded with a belt.
S. I. H. By care lay heavy sleep, the cousen of death,
Flat on the ground, and still as any stone:
A very corpse, save yielding forth a breath,
Small keep took he whom fortune frownd on,
Or whom she lifted up into the throne
Of high renown: but as a living death,
So dead alive, of life he drew the breath.
M. Sack. A drowsy head to earth by dull desire
Draws down the soul that should to heaven aspire.
Writing these later lines, weary well-nie
Of sacred Pallas, pleasing labor dear,
Mine humble chin saluteth often my breast,
With an Ambrosian deawe mine eyes possessed
By peece-meale close; all moving powers die still,
From my dull fingers drops my fainting quill.
Down in my sloath-bound bed again I shrink,
And in dark Laethe all deep cares I sink.
I. Syl. Solitariness.
Sweet solitary life thou true repose,
Wherein the wise contemplate heaven aright,
In thee no dread of war or worldly foes,
In thee no pomp seduceth mortal sight.
In thee no wanton ears to win with words,
Nor lurking toies which silly life affords.
D.L. Soldiers.
-- O Soldiers envy near ally to Kings
Maiesticke humor, careful jealous thought:
Thou, which awak'st us from ignoble things,
A passion nearest to a godhead brought.
Only indefinite: to whom none brings
Limit or bound, thou greater then our thought,
Who holds thee, holds a power to make him able,
Who looses then, becomes most miserable.
I. Mark, None is so poor of sense and eine,
To whom a soldier does not shine.
G. Chap. No elegancie can bewtifie
A shameless lump of gluttony:
His heart sweet Cupids tents reiects,
That only meat and drink affects.
O Flora all men's intellects,
Know soldiers power such respects,
Mere helps for need his mind suffices,
Dull sleep and surfets he despises:
Loves trumpe his temples exercises,
Courage and love his life compriseth.
Idem. Soul.
-- He that spread the skies
And fixed the earth, first formed the soul in man,
This true Prometheus first made men of earth,
And shead in him a beam of heavenly fire,
Now in their mothers wombes before their birth,
Does in all sons of men their souls inspire.
And as Minerua is in fables fainde
From Jove, without an other to proceed,
So our true Jove without an others aid,
Does daily millions of Mineruaes breed.
I Dauies. Like as the sun above the light does bring,
Though we behold it in the air below,
So from the eternal light the soul does spring,
Though in the body she her powers do show.
Idem. The soul a substance and a body is,
Which God himself does in the body make,
Which makes the man; or every man from this
The nature of a man and name does take.
And though the spirit be to the body knit,
As an apt mean her power to exercise:
Which are, life, motion, sense, and will and wit,
Yet she suruiues, although the body dies.
She is a substance and a real thing,
Which has it self an actual working might,
Which neither from the senses power does spring,
Nor from the bodies humors tempered right.
She is a vine which does no propping need,
To make her spread her self, or spring upright,
She is a star whose beams do not proceed
From any sin, but from a native light.
Idem. She is a spirit and an heavenly influence,
Which from the fountain of Gods spirit does flow,
She Is a spirit, yet not like air nor wind,
Nor like the spirits about the heart or brain,
Nor like the spirits which Alchimists definde,
When they in every thing seek gold in vain.
Idem. -- To show her powerful deity,
Her sweet Endimion more to beautify,
Into his soul the goddess does infuse,
The fiery Nature of an heavenly Muse:
Which the spirit laboring by the mind,
Partaketh of celestial things by kind:
For why the soul being divine alone,
Exempt from gross and vild corruption,
Of heavenly secrets incomprehensible,
Of which the dull flesh is not sensible*
And by one only powerful faculty,
Yet governs a multiplicitie,
Being essential, uniform in all,
Not to be severed or diuiduall:
But in her function holds her estate,
By powers divine in her ingenerate:
And so by inspiration conceiueth,
What heaven to her by divination breaths.
M. Drayton. Like as the soul does rule the earthly mass,
And all the service of the body frame,
So love of soul does love of body pass,
No less then perfect gold surmounts the meanest brass,
Ed. Spencer. Every good motion that the soul awakes,
A heavenly figure sees from whence it takes,
That sweetelesse bloome which by power of kind,
Forms like it self an image of the mind,
And in our faith the operations be,
Of that diuinesse which by faith we see,
Which never erres but accidentally,
By our frail fleshes in becilitie,
By each temptation ouer-apt to slide,
Except our spirit becomes our bodies guide.
For as our bodies prisons be the towers,
So to our souls these bodies be of ours,
Whose fleshly walls hinder that heavenly light,
As these stone walls deprive our wished sight.
Idem. -- As Phoebus throws
His beams abroad, though he in clouds be closed
Still glauncing by them till she find opposed
A loose and rorid vapor, that is fit
T'euent his searching beams, and uses it
To form a twenty colored eye,
Cast in a circle round about the sky.
So when our fiery soul, our bodies star,
(That ever is in motion circular)
Conceiues a form in seeking to display it,
Through all our cloudy parts it does convey it:
Forth at the eye, as the most pregnant place,
And that reflects it round about the face.
Idem. Like as the moisture which the thirsty earth
Sucks from the Sea to fill her empty veins,
From out her womb at last does take a birth,
And runs a nymph along the grassie plains:
Long does she stay, as loath to leave the land,
From whose soft side she first did issue make,
She tastes all places, turns to every hand,
Her flowing banks unwilling to forsake,
Yet nature so her streams does lead and carry,
As that her course does make no final stay,
Till she her self unto the Ocean marry,
Within whose watrie bosom first she lay.
Even so our soul within this earthly mould,
The spirit does secretly infuse,
Because at first she does the earth behold,
And only this material world she viewes.
At first our mother earth she holds dear,
And does embrace the world and worldly things,
She flies close to the ground and houers here,
And mounts not up with her celestial wings.
Yet under heaven she cannot light on ought,
That with her heavenly nature does agree,
She cannot rest, she cannot fix her thought,
She cannot in this world contented be.
I. Dauies. When the soul finds here no true content,
And like Noah's Dove, can no sure footing take,
She does return from whence she first was sent,
And flies to him that first her wings did make.
Idem. Heaven waxes old, and all the spheres above
Shall one day faint, and their swift motion stay,
And time it self shall cease in time to move,
Only the soul suruiues and lives for aye.
Idem. When as the soul is drowned once in vice,
The sweet of sin makes hell a Paradise*
M, Drayton, ^s is the fable of the Lady fair,
Which for her lust was turnde into a cow,
When thirsty to a stream she did repair,
And saw her self transformde she knew not how,
At first she startles, and she stands amazd,
And loathes the watery glass wherein she gazd:
At last for terror she from thence does fly,
And shunnes it still, though she for thirst do die.
Even so man's soul, which did Gods image bear,
And was at first fair, good, and spotless pure,
Since with her sins her beauties blotted were,
Does of all sights her own sight least endure:
For even at first reflecting she espies
Such strange Chimeraes and such monsters there,
Such toys, such antickes, and such vanities,
As she retyres, and shrinks for shame and fear.
I. Dauis. Even as the man loves least at home to be,
That has a sluttish house haunted with spirits,
So she impatient her own faults to see,
Turns from her self, and in strange things delights*
Idem. -- It Is a sacred cure
To salve the souls dread wounds, omnipotent
That nature is, that cures the impotent
Even in a moment, sure grace is infusde
By divine favor, nor by actions used:
Which is as permanent as heavens bliss,
To them that have it, then no habit is.
I. Marston. That learned Father which so firmly proves
The soul of man immortal and divine,
And does the several offices define.
Anima. Gives her that name as she the body moves.
Amor. Then is she love embracing charity.
Animus. Moving a will in us, it is the mind.
Men's. Retaining knowledge still the same in kind.
Memoria. As intellectual it is the memory.
Ratio. In judging, Reason only is her name.
Sensus. In speedy apprehension it is Sense.
Conscientia. In right or wrong men call her Conscience.
Spiritus. The Spirit, when to Godward it does inflame.
These of the soul the several functions be.
M. Drayton. Like as two bellows blown turn by turn,
By little and little make cold coals to burn,
And then their fire inflamde with glowing heat,
An iron bar which on the Anuile beat,
Seems no more iron, but flies almost all,
In hissing sparkles and quicke-bright cinders small.
So the worlds soul should in our soul inspire,
The Eternal force of an eternal fire,
And then our soul (as form) breathe in our corse,
Her countlesse numbers, and heavens turned force,
Wherewith our bodies beauty beautified,
Should like our (deathlesse soul) have never died.
I. Syluester. Of Sorrow.
In black all clad there fell before my face,
A ptiteous wight whom woe had all forewast,
Forth on her eyes the cristall tears out brast,
And sighing fore her hands she wrung and fold,
Tare all her hair, that ruth was to behold;
Her body small, sore withered and fore spent,
As is the stalk that summers drought oppressed,
Her welked face with woeful tears besprent:
Her color pale (as it seemed) her best,
In woe and plaint reposed was her rest:
And as the stone that drops of water wears,
So dented were her cheeks with fall of tears:
Her eyes swollen with flowing streams afloate,
Wherewith her looks thrown up full pitiously,
Her forcelesse hands together often she smote,
With doleful shrikes that ecchoed in the sky,
Whose plaints such sighs did strait accompany,
That in my doom was never man did see
A wight but half so woe-begone as she.
Sorrow I am, in endless sorrows pained,
Among the furies in the infernal lake,
Where Pluto God of hell so grisly black,
Does hold his throne and Laethes deadly taste,
Does riue remembrance of each thing fore-past.
M. Sackuile. Sorrows first leader of this furious crowde,
Muffled all over in a sable cloud,
Old before age, afflicted night and day,
Her face with wrinkles warped every way,
Creeping in corners, where she sits and vies;
Sighs from her heart, tears for her blubbered eyes,
Accompanied with selfe-consuming care,
With weeping pity, thought, and mad despair,
That bears about her burning coals and cords,
Aspes, poisons, pistols, haulters, knives, and swords,
Foul squinting envy, that selfe-eating elfe,
Through others leanness fatting up her self,
Joining in mischief, feeding but with langour,
And bitter tears, her toad-like swelling anger,
And jealousy that never sleeps for fear,
(Suspicious flea still nibling in her ear)
That leaves repast and rest, near pinde and blind,
With seeking what she would be loath to find.
I. Siluester. Two inward vulturs, sorrow and disdain.
Sorrow misfortunes son, dispayres foul fire.
Ed. Fairfax. Sorrow breaks seasons and reposing hours,
Makes the night morning, and the noone tide night,
W. Shakespeare. Sorrow is still unwilling to give over.
S. Daniell. Sorrow grows senseless when too much she bears.
M. Dr. Sad sorrow like a heavy ringing bell,
Once set in ringing, with his own weight goes,
Then little strength rings out the doleful knel.
W. Sh. It is some ease our sorrows to reveal,
If they to whom we shall impart our woes,
Seem but to feel a part of what we feel.
And meet us with a sigh but at a close.
S. Daniell. Sighs are the ease calamity affoords,
Which serve for speech when sorrow wants words.
Idem. Fell sorrows tooth never ranckles more,
Then when it bites, but launcheth not the sore.
Idem. -- Sorrow close shrouded in the heart.
I know to keep, it is a wondrous smart,
Each thing imparted, is more ease to bear,
When the rain is fallen, the clouds wax clear.
Ed. Spencer. -- Sorrow ne need be hastened on,
For he will come without calling anon.
Idem. -- Snarling sorrow has less powrc to bite
The man that mocks at it, and sets it light.
Ed. Spencer. He that his sorrow sought through wilfulness,
And his foe fettered would release again,
Deserves to taste his follies fruit, repented pain.
Ed. Spencer. -- Mirth does search the bottom of annoy,
Sad souls are slain in mirthie company,
Grief best is pleased with griefs society:
True sorrow then is feelingly suffizde,
When with like sorrow it is sympathizde.
True sorrow has not ever a wet eye.
Th. Dekker. Sad sorrow ever joys to hear her worst.
S. D. Suspicion.
-- False suspicion of another is
A sure condemning of our own amiss.
Edw. Gilpin. Mistrust does treason in the truest raise,
Suspicious Romulus stained his walls first reared
With brothers blood, whom for light leap he feared,
The jealous cuckold wears th'infamous horn,
So not in brotherhood, jealousy may be borne.
M. of M. Riualles in love will be suspicious quickly.
I. Weeuer. The Merchant traffiking abroad, suspects his wife at home
A youth will play the wanton, and a wanton prove a mome.
W. Warner. Tears.
-- These two parts belong
Unto true knowledge, words and tears have force,
To move compassion in the savage minds
Of brutish people reason wanting kinds.
Tho. Middleton. Tears, vows, and prayers gain the hardest hearts.
S. Daniell. Tears work no truce, but where the heart is tender.
D. Lodge. Tears harden lust, though marble wear with rain.
W. Sh. Seld speaks love, but sighs his secret pains,
Tears are his truch-men, words do make him tremble.
R. Green. Tears cannot soften flint, nor vows convert.
S. D. A doleful case desires a doleful song,
Without vain art, or curious complement,
And squallid fortune into baseness flung,
Does scorn the pride of wonted ornament.
Ed. Spencer. Temperance.
-- Temperance which golden squire,
Betwixt these two can measure out a mean,
Neither to melt in pleasures hot desire,
Nor fry in heartlesse grief and doleful teene,
Thrice happy man who faires them both a tweene.
Ed. Spencer. Who ever does to Temperance apply
His steadfast life, and all his actions frame,
Trust me shall find no greater enemy,
Then stubburne perturbation to the same:
To which right well the wise do give that name:
For it the goodly peace of stayed minds
Does overthrew, and troublous war proclaim,
His own woes author, who so bound it finds,
As did Pyrrhocles, and it wilfully unbind.
Idem. A harder lesson to learn continence,
In joyous pleasure then in grievous pain,
For sweetness does allure the weakest sense.
So strongly that uneath it can refrain,
From that which feeble nature couers feign,
But grief and wrath that be her enemies
And foes of life she better can restrain,
Yet virtue vaunts in both their victories.
Idem. O in what safety Temperance does rest,
When it finds harbor in a kingly breast.
M. Drayton. Of all Gods works which do this world adorn,
There is none more fair and excellent
Then is man's body, both for power and form,
Whilst it is kept in sober government:
But none then it more foul and indecent,
Distempered through misrules, and passions base,
It grows a monster, and incontinent,
Does loose his dignity and native grace.
Ed. Spencer. Thoughts.
Thoughts are the slaves of life, and life times fool,
And time that takes survey of all the world
Must have a stop.
W. Shakespeare. Thoughts are but dreams, till their effects be tried.
Idem. Who so thinks many things, brings few to a fortunate ending.
A. France. The feeble eyes of our aspring thoughts,
Behold things present, and record things past,
But things to come exceed our humane reach.
G. Peele. Unfeigned thoughts do seldom dream on evil.
Birds never limde no secret bushes fear.
W. Sh. If all men's thoughts were written in their face,
Some one that now the rest does ouercrow,
Some others ebb that wants his sovereigns grace,
When as the Prince their inward thoughts should know
The meaner then should take the better place,
The greatest man might stoop and sit below.
S. I. Harrington. Time.
Beauties great enemy, and to all the rest
That in the garden of Adonis springs,
Is wicked Time, who with his sith addressed,
Does mow the flowing herbs and goodly things,
And all their glory to the earth down flings,
Where they do wither, and are foully marde,
He flies about, and with his flaggie wings,
Beats down both leaves and buds without regard.
Ne ever pity may relent his malice hard.
E. Spencer. Misshapen Time, coapsmate of ugly might,
Swift subtle poast, carrier of grisly care,
Eater of youth, false slave to false delight,
Base watch of woes, sins packhorse, virtues snare,
Thou nursest all, and murtherest all that are.
W. Shakespeare. Stealing Time the subject to delay.
S. Ph. Sydney. -- Times golden thigh
Vpholdes the flowrie body of the earth,
In sacred harmony and every birth
Of men, audacious makes legitimate,
Being used aright, the use of times is fate.
G. Chapman. No mortal form that under moon remains,
Exempt from traitorous Time, continues one.
Now mountes the flood, and straight his waves restrains
Now flows the tide, and strait the sourse is gone,
Who toils by Sea, must choose the fairest gale,
For time abodes our good or badde avail*
D. Lodge. Al those that live and think themselves but slime,
Must choose and thrive by favor of the time.
Idem. Swift speedy Time, feathered with flying hours,
Dissolues the beauty of the fairest browe.
S. Daniell. Time does consume fame, honor, wit, and strength,
Time roots out youth and beauties look at length.
Tho. Watson. Time wanting bonds, still wants certainty.
M. Dr. To Fames rich treasure Time unlocks the door,
Which angry sorrow had shut up before.
Idem. Time is a bondslaue to eternity.
Tho. Kyd. All that does live is subject to his law,
All things decay in time, and to their end do draw.
Ed. Spencer. What wrong has not continuance out-worne,
Years makes that right that never was so borne.
S. Daniell. Good time is blessed, badde time we hold accursed,
Time hurts them often that he did help at first.
T. Churchyard. Times glory is to calm contending kings,
To unmask falsehood, and bring truth to light,
To stamp the seal of time in aged things,
To wake the morn, and sentinel the night,
To wrong the wronger till he render right:
To ruinate proud buildings with his hours,
And smeare with dust their glittering golden towers,
To fill with worm holes stately monuments,
To feed oblivion with decay of things,
To blot old Books, and alter their contents,
To pull the quilles from ancient Ravens wings,
To dry the old oaks sap, and cherish springs
To spoil antiquities of hammered steel,
And turn the giddie round of fortunes wheel,
To show the Beldame daughters of her daughters,
To make the child a man, the man a child,
To slay the tiger that does lively slaughter,
To tame the unicorn and the lion wild,
To make the subtle in themselves be guild,
To cheer the plowman with increasefull crops,
And waste huge stones with little water-drops.
W. Shakespeare. Truth.
The Truth does does dwell within the holy tables,
Of Gods live word, not in our wanton brain,
Which daily coining some strange error vain,
For gold takes lead, for truth electeth Fables.
I. Siluester. Truth is no harauld, nor no so sophist sure,
She notes not men's names, their sheelds or crests,
Though she compare them unto birds and beasts,
But whom she does fore-shew shall reign by force,
She terms a woolfe, a dragon, or a bear,
A wilful Prince, a raignelesse raging horse,
A boar, a lion, a coward much in fear,
A hare or heart, a crafty pricked ear,
A lecherous, a bull, a goat, a foale,
An underminer, a mould-warpe, or a moale.
M. of M. -- Tried truth
Does best be seem a simple naked tale,
Ne needs to be with painted process pricked,
That in her self has no diversity,
But always shows one vndisguised face,
Where deep deceit and lies must seek her shade,
And wrappe their words in guilefull eloquence,
As ever fraught with contrariety.
G. Gascoigne. The truth has certain bounds, but falsehood none.
S. Daniell. -- The naked truth is a well-clothed lie,
A nimble quick pale meunts to dignity
By force or fraud, that matters not a jot,
So massy wealth may fall unto thy lot.
Io. Marston. Treason.
Conspiracy against the person of a Prince,
Is treason against the deity of heaven.
Th. Achellye. -- Treason is but trusted like the Fox,
Who nere so tamde, so cherisht, and locked up,
Will have a wild trick of his auncetors.
W. Sh. No virtue merits praise once touched with blot of treason.
S. Ph. Sydney. Who fails one is false, though trusty to another*
Idem. There is no treason wounds half so deep,
As that which does in Princes bosom sleep.
M. Drayton. Who that resists his dread sovereign Lord,
Does damn his soul by Gods own very word,
A Christian subject should with honor due,
Obey his sovereign though he were a Jew,
Whereby assured when subjects do rebel,
Gods wrath is kindled, threatening fire and hell.
M. of M. Was never rebel before the world and since,
That could or should prevail against his Prince.
Idem. Revolted subjects of themselves will quail.
I. Syluester. Tyranny.
-- Next to Tyranny
Comes wars, discention, civil mutiny.
Chapter Middl. In greatest wants t'inflict the greatest woe,
This is the worst that tyranny can show.
Idem. Hell haleth tyrants down to death amain,
Was never yet, nor shall be cruel deed
Vnquited left, but had as cruel meed.
M. of M. -- No tyrant commonly
Living ill can kindly die,
But either traitorously surprizde
Does coward poison quayle their breath,
Or their people have deuizde,
Or their Guard to seek their death.
Tho. Kyd. It is an hell in hateful vassallage,
Under a tyrant to consume ones age,
A selfe-shauen Dennis, or an Nero fell,
Whose cursed Courts with blood and incest swell:
An Owl that flies the light of Parliaments
And state assemblies, jealous of th'intents
Of Private tongues, who for a pastime sets
His Peers at odds, and on their fury whets,
Who neither faith, honor, nor right respects.
Idem. Virtue.
What one art thou thus in torn weed yclad?
Virtue, in price, whom ancient sages had:
Why poorly clad? for fading goods past care:
Why double faced? I mark each fortunes rare:
This bridle what? minds rages to restrain:
Why bear you tools? I love to take great pain:
Why wings? I teach above the stars to fly:
Why tread you death? I only cannot die.
S. Th. Wiat. The path that leads to Virtues Court is narrow,
Thornie, and up a hill, a bitter journey:
But being gone through, you find al heavenly sweets,
Th'entrance is all flintie, but at the end
Two Towers of pearls and cristall you ascend.
Th. Dekkar. Virtue is fairest in a poor art aye.
Idem. Virtue abhorres too wear a borrowed face.
Idem. The wisest scholar of the wight most wise,
By Phoebus doom, with sugared sentence says,
That virtue If it once meet with our eyes,
Strange flames of love it in our souls would raise.
S. Ph. Sydney. That grows apace, that virtue helps t'aspire.
M. R^^don. When virtue rises, base affections fall.
Ed. Fairfax. Like as the horse well mand abides the bit,
And learnes his stop by rain in riders hand,
Where mountain colt that is not sadled yet,
Runs headlong on amidst the fallowed land,
Whose fierce resist scarce bendes with any band.
So men reclaimed by virtue tread aright,
Where led by follies, mischiefs on them light.
D. Lodge. Virtue does ^urb affection, and for conscience flies sin,
To leave for imperfection fear or shame no praise does win.
W. Warner. Virtue it self turns vice, being misapplyed,
And vice sometime by action dignified.
W. Shakespeare. Virtue in greatest danger is most shown,
And though oppressed, yet nere is overthrown.
S. Daniell. In virtue it is said, that men themselves suruie.
W. W. Honor indeed, and all things yield to death,
(Virtue excepted) which alone suruiues,
And living toyleth in an earthly gaile,
At last to be extolled in heavens high joys.
T. Kyd. All things decay, yet virtue shall not die,
This only gives us immortallitie.
M. Drayton. Whence is it that the flower of the field does fade,
And lies buried long in winters bale,
Yet soon as spring his mantle does display,
It flowreth fresh, as it should never fail,
But thing on earth that is of most avail.
As virtues and beauties bud,
Releeuen not for any good,
The branch once dead, the bud needs eke must quail*
Ed. Spencer. All that we had, or mortal men can have,
Seems only hut a shadow from the grave,
Virtue alone lives still.
Th. Dekkar. Virtue is more amiable and more sweet,
When virtue and true majesty do meet.
E. Spencer. -- All the sorrow in the world is less
Then virtues might and valures confidence,
For who will bide the burden of distress,
Must not here think to live, for life is wretchedness.
Idem. Virtue makes honor, as the soul does sense,
And merit, far exceedes inheritance.
G. Chapman. -- Virtue of the ^uncient blood and kin,
Does only please the parties she is in.
M. of M. -- Only virtue nobleness does dignifie,
And vicious life a lineage base does signify.
S. I. Harrington. The simple virtue may consist alone,
But better are two virtues joined in one.
D. Lodge. What virtue gets, once got does never waste,
And having this, this thou for ever haste.
M. Dryaton. Joy graven in sense, like snow in water wastes,
Without preserve of virtue nothings lasts.
G. Chapman. Virtue obscurde yeeldes small and happy gains,
But actiuely employed, she worth retains.
D. Lodge. What virtue breeds, iniquity deuours,
We have no good at all that we can say is ours*
But ill annexed opportunity,
Or killes his life or else his quality.
W. Sh. Virtue dies not, her tomb we need not raise,
Let them trust tombs which have out-liu'd their praise.
Th. Bastard. Vice.
Vice rides a horseback, Virtue does from out the saddl^ boult.
W. Warner. What licour first the earthen pot does take,
It keeps still the savor of the same,
Full hard it is a camocke straight to make,
Or crooked logges with wainscot fine to frame,
It Is hard to make the cruel Tiger tame:
And so it fares with those have vices caught,
Naught (once they say) and ever after naught.
M. of M. Although that virtue often wants due reward,
Yet seldom vice wants due deserved blame,
S. I. H. Where vice is countenanced with Nobility,
Art clean excluded, ignorance held in,
Blinding the world with mere hypocrisy,
Yet must be soothed in all their slavish sin,
Great malcontents to grow they then begin,
Nursing vild wits to make their factious tools,
Thus mighty men often prove the mightiest fools.
M. Drayton. Victory.
With victory revenge does ever cease,
S. I. H. He lives long that lives victorious.
Th. Kyd. The victor can no honor justly claim,
To loose the men who should advance the same.
-- That fisher is not fine,
Who for a frogge will loose a golden line:
The holy head-band seems not to attire
The head of him, who in his furious ire,
Preferres the pain of those that have him teend,
Before the health and safety of one friend.
Tho. Hudson. Vain is the vaunt and victory unjust,
That more to mighty hands then rightful cause does trust.
Edw. Spencer. Loss is no shame, nor to be less then foe,
But to be lesser then himself, does mar
Both loosers lotte, and victors praise also,
Vain others overthrows, who self does overthrow.
Idem. Understanding.
Most miserable creature under sky
Man, without understanding does appear,
For all this worlds affliction he thereby,
And fortvnes freates is wisely taught to bear:
Of wretched life the only joy she is,
And the only comfort in calamity,
She arms the breast with constant patience,
Against the bitter throws of dolours darts,
She solaceth with rules of sapience,
The gentle minds in midst of worldly smarts,
When he is sad, she seeks to make him merry,
And does refresh his spirits when they be weary,
Ed. Spencer. Vows.
Good vows are never broken with good deeds,
For then good deeds were bad: vows are but seeds,
And good deeds fruits.
G. Chapman. We know not how to vow, till love vnblind us,
And vows made ignorantly never bind us.
Idem. Our vows must be performed to God and King.
M. Drayton. -- A promise made for fear is void.
S. I. H. -- A man such promise must forsake,
As at the first unlawful was to make.
Idem. Virginity.
Like to the Rose I count the virgin pure,
That grows on native stem in garden fair,
Which whiles it stands with walls enuiron'd sure,
Where herd-men with their herds cannot repair
To savor it, it seems to allure
The morning dew, the heat, the earthly air,
Gallant young men and lovely dames delight
By their sweet sent, and in their pleasing sight:
But when that once it is gathered and gone
From proper stalk, where late before it grew,
The love the liking little is or none,
Favor, and grace, beauty and all adue:
So when a virgin graunts to one alone,
The precious flower for which so many sue:
Well he that gets it may love her best,
But she forgoes the love of all the rest.
S. I. H. Jewels being lost, are found again, this never,
It Is lost but once, and once lost, lost for ever.
Chapter Marlow. Virginity though praised is like a bird, for why,
As much the flesh is frail therein, as in the fear to die,
What was it said to all but us increase and multiply?
W. Warner. -- Virginity
Is neither essence subject to the eye,
No nor to any one exterior sense,
Nor has it any place of residence,
Nor is it of earth or mould celestial,
Or capable of any form at all.
Chapter Marlow. I know not her that willingly with maidenhead would die.
W. W. Use.
Use make things nothing huge, and huge things nothing.
G. Chapman. Foul cankering rust the hidden treasure frets.
But gold that's put to use, more gold begets.
W. Sh. War.
Lastly stood war in glistering arms yclad,
With visage grimme, stern looks and ghastly hood,
In his right hand a naked sword he had,
That to the hilts was all with blood imbrude,
And in his left that kings and kingdoms rued,
Famine and fire he had, and there withal
He rased towns, and threw down towers and all,
Cities he sacked, and realms that whilome flowred
In honor, glory, and rule above the best,
He ouerwhhlm'd, and all their fame devoured,
Consumde, destroyde, wasted, and never ceast,
Till he therewith their name and all oppressed:
His face forhued with wounds, and by his side,
There hung his targe with gashes deep and wide,
In midst of which depainted there we find
Deadly debate, all full of snakie hair,
That with a bloody fillet was ybound,
Out-breathing noght, but discord every where.
M. Sackuille. The Poets old in their fond fables faind,
That mighty Mars is God of war and strife:
The Astronomers think that whereas Mars does reign
That all debate and discord must be rife:
Some think Bellona, Goddess of that life.
Among the rest that Painter had some skill,
Which thus in arms did once set out the same,
A field of gules, and on a golden hill.
A stately town consumed all with flame,
On chief of sable taken from the dame,
A sucking babe ( O) borne to bide mischance,
Begoard with blood, and pierced with a lance.
On high the Helm, I bear it well in mind,
The wreath was silver powdered all with shot,
About the which (goutte du sang) did twind.
A roll of sable black, and foul beblot,
The crest two hands, which may not be forgot,
For in the right a trenchand blade did stand,
And in the left a fiery burning brand.
G. Gascoigne. -- War the mistress of enormitie,
Mother of mischief, monster of deformity,
Laws, manners, arts, she breaks, she marres, she chaces,
Blood, tears, bowres, towers, she spils, smites, burns, and races,
Her brazen teeth shake all the earth asunder:
Her mouth a firebrand, and her voice a thunder,
Her looks are lightning, every glaunce a flash,
Her fingers guns, that all to powder pash,
Fear and despair, flight and disorder, coast
With hasty march before her murderous host,
As burning, waste, rape, wrong, impiety,
Rage, ruins, discord, horror, cruelty,
Sack, sacrilege, impunitie pride,
Are still stern consorts by her barbarous side.
And poverty, sorrow, and desolation,
Follow her armies bloody transmigration.
I^ Siluester. O war begot in pride and luxury,
The child of wrath and of dissension*
Horrible good, mischief necessary,
The foul reformer of confusion:
Unjust just, scourge for our iniquity.
Cruel recurer of corruption.
S. Daniell. O goodly usage of those anticke times,
In which the sword was servant unto right,
When not for malice and contentious crimes,
But all for praise, and proof of manly might,
The martial brood accustomed to fight:
Then honor was the meede of victory,
And yet the vanquished had no dispight,
Let later age, that now the use envy,
Vild rancour so avoid and cruel furquedry.
Ed. Spencer. War rightly handled is most excellent,
And easy makes impossibility:
It mounts the Alps, and through the seas does rent*
By it in blood a way to heaven we see.
I. Markham. Under wars brazen feet stoopes all the earth,
His mouth a flaming brand, his voice a thunder,
Idem. No war is right, but that which needful is,
S. Daniell. The God of war has many men in store,
Which wait always to keep his kingdom up,
Of whom no one does show his service more,
Then lingering hope, which still does be^re the cup,
And flatteringly lendes every man a sup,
Which haunts his course, or in his progesse pass,
Hope brings the bowl wherein they all must quaffe,
G. Gascoigne. War seems sweet to such as raunge it not.
Idem. Men know not War, nor rightly how to deem it,
That first by War have not been taught t'esteeme it.
S. I. H. -- Wise men ever have preferred far,
Th'vniustest peace, before the iustest War.
S. Daniell. -- Time observing providence and War,
Still makes their foes far stronger then they are.
Idem. Sad be the sights and bitter fruits of War,
And thousand furies wait on wrathful sword,
Ne ought the praise of prowess more does mar,
Then foul revenging rage and proud contentious jar.
Ed. Spencer. -- Great reuenew
The chiefest sinew unto War affoords.
D. Lodge. -- Wars that public good pretend,
Work most in justice being done for spite,
For th'agreeued evermore do bend,
Against those whom they see of greatest might,
Who though themselves are wronged and often forced,
Yet though they can do most are thought the worst.
S. Daniell. -- Mars is Cupidoes friend
And is for Venus love renouned more,
Then all the wars and spoils the which he did before.
Ed. Spencer. Will.
From idle wit, there springs a braine-sicke will,
With wise men lust, which foolish make a God,
This in the shape of virtue reigns still.
D. Lodge. Will puts in practice what the wit deuiseth.
Will ever acts, and wit contemplates still,
And as from wit the power of wisdom rises,
All other virtues daughters are of will.
Idem. Will is the Prince, and wit the counselor,
Which does for common good in council sit,
And when wit is resolved, will lends her power,
To execute what is devised by wit.
I. Dauies. Will is as free as any Emperor,
Naught can restrain her gentle liberty,
No tyrant nor no torrent has the power
To make us will when we unwilling be.
Idem. Even as the will should goodness truly know,
We have a will which that true good should choose
Although will often, when wit false forms does show,
Take ill for good, and good for ill refuse.
Idem. It lives not in our power to love or hate,
For will in us is ouer-rul'd by fate.
Chapter Marlow. A stronger hand restraines our wilful powers,
A will must rule above this will of ours,
Not following what our vain desires do woe,
For virtues sake, but what we only do.
M. Dr. -- Headless will true judgment does ensnare.
Idem. Self-will does frown, when honest zeal reproues,
Idem. Whereas our actions measure no regard,
Our lawless will is made his own reward.
M. Dra. -- With a world of mischiefs and offense,
Unbridled will rebels against the sense.
D. Lodge. He least should list that may do what he will.
S. Dan. Wisdom.
Our God himself for wisdom most is praised,
And men to God thereby are nighest raised.
Ed. Spencer. Wisdom does warn, whilst foe is in the gate.
To stay the step, ere forced to retreat.
Idem. Wisdom must judge twixt men apt to amend,
And minds incurable borne to offend.
S. D. -- In danger wisdom does advise,
In humble terms to reconcile our foes.
D. Lodge. -- Wisdom and the sight of heavenly things,
Shines not so clear as earthly vanities.
G. Chapman. It Is said a wise man all mishaps withstands,
For though by stars we borne to mischiefs are,
Yet prudence bailes us quite from careful bands.
M. of M. Foresight does still on all advantage lie.
Wise men must give place to necessity.
M. Dr. -- A wiseman poor
Is like a sacred Book that is never read,
T'himselfe he lives, and to all else seems dead:
This age thinks better of a gilded fool,
Then of thread bare saint in wisdoms school.
Th. Dekkar. Wise men let faults ore-passe, they cannot mend.
Chapter Middle. Who can themselves beware by others costs,
May be accounted well among the wise.
S. I. H. -- Whatsoever Stars seem to importune,
Wisdom predominates both fate and fortune.
Chapter Fitz Griffon. Wit.
The wit the pupil of the souls clear eye,
And in man's world the only shining star,
Looks in the mirror of the fantasy,
Where all the gathering of the senses ate,
From thence this power the shape of things abstract^
And them within her passive part receives,
Which are enlightened by that part which acts,
And so the form of single things receives:
But after by discoursing to and fro,
Anticipating and comparing things
She does all universal natures know,
And all effects into their causes bring.
Our wit is given Almighty God to know,
Our will is given to love him being known,
But God could not be known to us below,
But by his works, which through the sense are known.
I. Dauis. Wit is the minds chief judge, which does control,
Of fancies Court the judgments false and vain,
Will, holds the royal scepter in the soul,
And on the passions of the heart does reign.
Idem. Emulation the proud nurse of wit.
S. D. -- Wit and learning are two Angels wings,
By which mean men soar up to mighty things.
Chapter Middl. Wit is with boldness prompt, with terror daunted,
And grace is sooner got of dames then granted.
Ed. Spencer. Some loose their wit with love, some with ambition
Some running to the sea great wealth to get,
Some following Lords and men of high condition,
Some in fair iewelles, rich and costly set.
One has desire to prove a rare magician,
Others with Poetry their wit forget:
Another thinks to be an Alchimist,
Till all he spent, and he his number mist.
S. I. H. Man's wit is monstrous, when the same from virtue does decline.
W. Warner. Man's wit does build for time but to devour,
But Virtue's free from time and fortunes power.
M. Dr. The wit not hurt, because not used more,
Grows dull and far less toward then before.
-- Wits ambition longs to the best,
For it desires in endless bliss to dwell.
I. Dauis. Best loves are lost for wit, when men blame fortune.
G. Chapman. -- Careless wit is wanton bewties page.
D. Lodge. The finest wits are soonest snarde with love.
Th. Achellye. A settled brain is worth a world of wit.
Th. Storer. Wits want makes men desirous to seem wise.
Woe.
Woe all in black within her hands did bear,
The fatal torches of a funeral,
Her cheeks were wet, dispersed was her hair,
Her voice was shrill (yet loathsome therewithal)
D. Lodge. Short time seems long in sorrows sharp sustaining,
Though woe be heavy, yet it seldom sleeps,
And they that watch see time how slow it creepes.
W. Shakespeare. -- Fellowship in woe, does woe assuage,
As palmers that make short their pilgrimage.
Idem. It Is double death to drown in ken of shore,
He ten times pines, that pines behoulding food:
To see the salve does make the wound ache more,
Great griefs grieve most at that would do it good,
Dear woes roll forward like a gentle flood:
Who being stopped, the bounden banks ore flows,
Grief dallied with, nor law nor limmit knows.
Idem. Distress likes dumps, when time is kept with tears.
Idem. For stronger woe we hardly long may wrest,
The depth of grief with words is sounded least.
M. Dra. -- The Painter
Who thought his colors pale could not declare
The special woe King Agamemnon bare,
When sacrificed was his only rage,
With bend of black he bound the fathers face.
Th. Hudson. Words.
-- Words
Windy atturnies of our clyent woes,
Ayery succeeders of intestate joys,
Poor breathing Orators of miseries,
Let them have scope, though what it does impart
Help not at all, yet does it ease the heart.
VV. Sh. Words are the tennants of an itching toy.
D. Lodge. Allusion of words is no sure ground,
For one thereon a steddie work to found.
One word of woe another after traineth.
S Ph. Sydney. -- Few words shall fit the trespass best,
Where no excuse can give the fault amending.
W. Sh. Deep sounds make better noise then shallow fords,
And sorrow ebbes being blown with wind of words.
W. Sh. Words are but wind, why cost they then so much,
The guilty kick when they too smartly to^ch.
Idem. Forth irreturnable flies the spoken word,
Be it in scoff, in earnest, or in bourd,
Without return and vnreceiu'd it hangs,
And at the takers mercy or rigor stands:
Which if he sowrely wrest, with wrathful cheer,
The shiuering word turns to the hearers fear:
If friendly courtesy do the word expound,
To th'speakers comfort quickly it does redound.
Idem. Smoothe words dissolve hard stones, fair words enforce
Pity in flintie hearts.
Chapter Middl. Through the world if it were sought,
Fair words enough a man should find,
They be good cheap, they cost right naught,
Their substance is but only wind:
But well to say, and so to mean,
That sweet accord is seldom seen.
S. Th. W. -- Words well placed move things were never thought.
G. Chapman. Even as the vapor which the fire repelles,
Turns not to earth, but in mid-ayre dwelles,
Where while it hangs, if Boreas frostie flawes,
With rigor rattle it: not to rain it thawes,
But thunder, lightning, rattling, hayle, or snow,
Sends down to earth, whence first it rose below.
But if fair Phoebus with his countenance sweet
Resolve it, down the dew or Manna sleete:
The Manna dew that in the Esterne lands,
Excelles the labor of the Bees small hands,
Else for her Memnon, gray Auroraes tears,
On the earth it stilleth the partner of her fears,
Or sends sweet showers to glad their mother earth
Whence first they took their first in constant birth.
To those great greefes ill taken words do grow,
Of words well taken such delights do flow.
M. of M. -- Men do foulest when they finest speak.
S. Daniell. They wash a Moore, they strive to dry the seas,
And plain proud Atlas, that intend to please
By filthy words, by railing, and detraction,
Proper to Momus, and his hateful faction:
For when they think they have deserved most,
Alas says wisdom, all the toil is lost.
D. Lodge. Few words well coucht, do most content the wise.
R. Green. Rash words flow from an unadvised mind.
Who once has past the bounds of honesty
In earnest deeds, may pass it well in words.
G. G. Have care to whom, of whom, and what to speak, though speech be true
That miss made Phoebus contrary his raue^s swan-like hue.
W. W. If so the crow would feast him without prate,
More meat he should receive, less brawle and hate
A fool he is that comes to preach and prate,
When men with swords their right and wrong debate.
-- Words well disposed,
Have secret power to appease inflamed rage.
Ed. Sp. Women.
-- Women be
Framde with the same parts of the mind as we,
Nay nature triumphed in their beauties birth,
And women made the glory of the earth:
The life of beauty, in whose supple breasts,
And in her fairest lodging virtue rests,
Whose towering thoughts attended with remorse,
Do make their fairness be of greater force.
I. Weeuer. What art so deep, what science is so hie,
Unto the which women have not attained,
Who list in stories old to look, may try
And find my speech herein nor false nor feigned,
And though of late they seem not to come nie
The praise their sex in former times have gained.
Doubtless the fault is either in back-biters,
Or want of skill or judgment in their writers.
Idem. Among the many rare and special gifts,
That in the female sex are found to sit,
This one is chief, that they at meerest shifts,
Give best advise, and show most ready wit,
But man except he chewes and thinks, and fifts,,
How every part may answer to their fit,
By rash advise does often ouer-shoote him,
And does accept the things that do no^boote him.
Idem. Those virtues that in women merit praise,
Are sober shows without, chaste thoughts within,
Truth says, and due obedience to their make,
And of their children honest care to take.
S. I. H. Let woolues and beasts be cruel in their kind,
But women meek, and have relenting minds.
M. Drayton. Not women, but our wilfulness does work our woe unrest
Though beauty, love, and they bear fault, we may abuse the best.
W. W. -- Men have Marble, women waxen minds
And therefore are they formed as Marble will,
The weak oppressed, the impression of strange kinds,
Is formed in them by force, by fraud, or skill,
Then call not them the Authors of their ill,
No more then wax shall be accounted evil,
Wherein is stamped the semblance of the devil.
Their smoothenesse like a goodly champaine plain,
Lays open all the little worms that creep,
In men as in a rough grown grove remain,
Caue-keeping euilles, that obscurely sleep,
Through cristall walls each little moule will peep,
Though men can cover minds with bold stern looks
Pale women's faces are their own faults Books.
No man inueyes against the withered flower,
But chides rough winter that the flower has killed,
Not that deuourd, but that which does devour,
Is worthy blame, O let it not be hild,
Poor women's faults, that they are so fulfilled,
With men's abuses those proud lores to blame,
Make weake-made women tenants to their shame.
W. Shakespeare. Be not therefore too proud and full of scorn,
O women-kind, that men come of your seed,
The fragrant Rose grows on the pricking thorn,
The Lilly sayre comes of a filthy weed,
In loathsome soil men sow the wholesome corn,
The basest mould the fairest flower does breed,
Ungrateful, false, crafty you are, and cruel,
Borne of our burning hell to be the fuel.
S. I. H. Base bullion for the stamp sake we allow,
Even so for men's impression do we you,
By which alone our reverend fathers say,
Women receive perfection every way.
Chapter Marlow. Their Virtues mount like billows to the skies,
And vanish straight out of the gazers eyes,
Hate and disdain is painted in their eyes,
Deceit and treason in their bosom lies.
G. Chapman. Women were made for this intent, to put us unto pain,
Yet sure I think they are a pleasure to the mind,
A joy which man can never want, as nature has assigned.
Idem, Extremely mad the man I surely deem,

For who wots not that women's subtilties

Idem. Such is the cruelty of women-kind,
When they have shaken off the shame-fac't band,

To purchase a licencious liberty.

That they were borne to base humility,
Unless the heavens them lift to lawful soueraintie.

H. C. Of Wrath,
-- Fierce revenging wrath
Rides on a Lion, loath for to be led,
And in his stand a burning brand he had,
The which he brandished about his head,
His eyes did hurl forth sparkles fiery red,
And stared stern on all that him beheld,
As ashes pale of hew, and seeming dead,
And on his dagger still his hand he held,
Trembling through hasty rage when choler in him sweld.
Ed. Spencer. -- Boiling wrath, stern, cruel, swift, and rash,
That like a boar her teeth does grind and gnash,
Whose hair does stare like bristled po^cupine,
Who sometimes rowles her ghastly glowing eyene,
And sometimes fixly on the ground does glaunce,
Now bleake, then bloody in her countenance,
Rauing and railing with a hideous sound,
Clapping her hands, stamping against the ground,
Bearing Bocconi, fire, and sword, to slay
And murder all that for her pity pray,
Banning her self to bane her enemy,
Disdaining death, provided others die,
Like falling towers o're-turned by the wind,
That break themselves on that they vndergrinde.
I. Syluester. Full many mischiefs follow cruel wrath,
As horrid bloodshed, and tumultuous strife,
Unmanly murder, and unthrifty scath,
Bitter despite, and rancors rustie knife,
And fretting grief, the enemy of life,
All these and many euilles more haunt ire,
The swelling spleen, and frenzy raging rife,
The shaking palsy, and Saint Fraunces fire.
Ed. Spencer. When men with wrath and sudden pains of ire,
Suffer themselves to be o're-whelm'd and drowned,
And hot revenge that burns l^ke flaming fire,
Moo^es hearts to hurt, or tongs or hands to wound,
Though after to a mend, if they desire,
Yet place of pardon seldom can be found.
S. I. H. What iron band, or what sharp hard-mouthed bitte,
What chain of Diamond (if such might be)
Can bridle wrathfulnesse, and conquer it,
And keep him in his bounds and due degree.
Idem. -- Hasty wrath and heedlesse hazardie,
Do breed repentance and lasting infamy.
Ed. Spencer. Poor silly lambs the Lion never tears,
The feeble Mouse may lie among great Bears,
But wrath of man his rancour to requite,
Forgets all reason, ruth, and mercy quite.
M. of M. -- He is a mad man that does seek

She comes unsought, and shunned follows eke:

Woe never wants when every cause is caught,
And rash occa^ion makes unquiet life.

Except he hald and tare him all beside.

T. Lodge. World,
The antique world in his first flowring youth,
Found no defect in his creators grace,
But with glad thanks and unreproved truth,
The gifts of sovereign bounty did embrace,
Like angels life was then man's happy case:
But later ages pride like corne-fed steed,
Abvsde her plenty and fatswoln increase,
To all licencious lust, and began exceed,
The measure of her mean and natural first seed.

Chapter Middl. O vain worlds glory, and uncertain state,
Of all that lives on face of sinful earth,
Which from their first until their utmost date,
Taste no one hour of happiness or mirth,
But like as is the ingate of their birth.
They crying creep out of their mothers womb,
So wayling back, go to their careful tomb.
Ed. Spencer. Ah wretched world, the den of wretchedness,
Deformd with filth and foul iniquity,

Filled with the wreakes of mortal misery,
Oh wretched world and all that is therein,
The vassals of Gods wrath, and slaves to sin.
Idem. -- O worlds inconstancy,
That which is firm does flit and fall away,
And that is flitting does abide and stay.
Idem. Must not the world wend in his common course,

Idem. This golden age to iron does decline,
As summer unto winter must resign.

Th. Bastard. Youth.
Youth is a bubble blown up with a breath,
Whose wit is weakness, and whose wage is death,
Whose way is wildnes, and whose Inn penance,
And stoop gallant age, the host of greeuance.
Ed. Spencer. If crooked age accounts youth his spring,
The spring the fairest season of the year,
Enricht with flowers, and sweetes, and many a thing
That fair and glorious to the eye appears:
It fits that youth the spring of man should be,
Richt with such flowers as virtue gets thee.
R. Green. For noble youth there is no thing so meet
As learning is, to know the good from ill,
To know the tongues, and perfectly endite,
And of the laws to have the perfect skill
Things to reform as right and justice will:
For honor is ordained for no cause,
But to see right maintained by the laws.
M. of M. The youth of Princes have no bounds for sin,
Unless themselves do make the bounds within.
S. Daniell. Most true it is, as vessels of first licours ever taste,
Love seasoned so with sweetness of youth, the same does ever last.
W. Warner. Like as the vessel ever bears a taste
Of the same juice wherewith it first was filled,
And as in fruitful ground the seed grows fast,
That first is sown after the ground is tilled:
So look what lore in youthful years is plast,
By that they grow the worse or better willed,
When as they came to manly age and stature,
Since education is another nature.
S. I. H. The tun retains long the taste and sent,

In age our reason hardly will restrain,

I. Weeuer. In grained habits died with often dips

I. Murston. The plowman first his land does dress and turn,
And makes it apt or ere the seed he sow,
Whereby he is full like to reap the corn,
Where otherwise no seed but weed should grow:
By which example men may easily know,
When youth have wealth before they can well use it,
It is no wonder though they do abuse it.

Th. Churchyard. The division of the day natural. Mediae noctis inclinatio.
Night was far spent, and now in Ocean deep,
Orion flying fast from hissing snake,
His flaming head did hasten for to steep.

By this the Northern Wagoner had set
His seuen-fold teeme behind the steadfast star,
That was in Ocean waves, yet never wet,

To all that in the wide deep wandering are,

Ed. Spencer. What time the native Bel-man of the night,

Idem. The cheerful cock, the sad nights trumpeter,
Waiting upon the rising of the sun,
Does sing to see how Cynthia shrinks her horn,
Where Clitia takes her progress to the East,
Where wringing west with drops of silver dew,
Her wonted tears of love she does renew,
The wandering swallow with her broken song,
The country wench unto her work awakes,
Whilst Cytherea sighing, walks to seek,
Her murdered love transformed to a rose,
Whom though she see, to croppe she kindly fears
But kissing sighs, and dewes him with her tears.
Th. Kyd. Now ere the purple dawning yet did spring,

And now the cock the mornings trumpeter,
Played hunts up, for the day star to appear,
Down slideth Phebe from her cristall chair,
S'daigning to lend her light unto the air.

M. Dra. Noctis concubium.
Now was the heavenly vault depriude of light
With suns depart, and now the darkness of the night,
Did light those beamy stars which greater lite did dark
Now each thing that inioyd that fiery quickening spark
(Which life is called) were moud their spirits to repose,
And wanting use of eyes, their eyes began to close:
A silence sweet, each where with one consent imbrast,
A music sweet, to one in careful musing plast:
And mother earth now clad in morning weed, did breathe
A dull desire to kiss the image of our death.
S. Ph. Sydney. It was the time, when rest soft sliding down
From heavens height, into man's heavy eyes,

Ed. Spencer. -- The sun already sanke

Ed. Fairfax. Now from the fresh, the soft, and tender bed,
Of her still mother gentle night out-flew

And sweete-breath Zephire on his spreading wings
Sleep, ease, repose, rest, peace, and quiet brings,

They softly dip in mild obliuions lake.

Ed. Spencer. Midnight was come, when every vital thing,
With sweet sound sleep their weary limbs did rest,
The beasts were still, the little birds that sing,
Now sweetly slept besides their mothers breast,
The old and all were snrowded in their rest,
The waters calm, the cruel seas did cease,
The woods, the fields, and all things held their peace
The golden stars were whi^ld amidst their race,
And on the earth did laugh with twinkling light,
When each thing nestled in his resting place,
Forgot days pain with pleasure of the night,
The hare had no the greedy hounds in sight,
The fearful Dear of death stood not in doubt,
The Partrich dreamd not of the falchens foot,
The ugly bear now minded not the stake,
Nor how the cruel mastiffes do her tear,
The stagge lay still vnroused from the brake,
The foamie bore feared not the hunters spear,
All things were still in desert, bush and breere:
The quiet heart now from their travails rest,
Soundly they slept in most of all their rest.

I. Markham. With falling mists the darkesome night extended

To cheerful minds that brings wanton sleep,
With many a fantasy and deluding toy,
And pensive heart it does delaie and keep
From tedious company, that would annoy,
Dull Saturnists that have abiurdall joy.
Th. Storer. Now spread the night her spangled canopie,
