Ed. Spencer. Herbs.
The sable Henbane, Morrell making mad,
Cold poisoning Poppey, itching, drowsy, sad.
The stifning Carpese, th'eyes-foe Hemlock stinking,
Limb-numming, belching: and the sinew shrinking.
Dead-laughing Apium, weeping Aconite.
(Which in our vulgar deadly Wolfes Bane hight)
The dropsie-breeding, sorrow-bringing Psyllie,
(Here called Fleawurt) Colchis banefull Lilly.
(With us wild-Saffran) blistering, biting fell,
Hot Napell, making lips and tongue to swell.
Blood-boyling Yew, and costiue Misseltoe,
With yee-cold Mandrake.
I. Syluester. * -- Through crooked woods he wandreth,
Round-winding rings, and intricate Meaanders,
False guiding paths, doubtful beguiling straies.
And right strong errors of an endless maze.
Idem. * There springs the shrub tree foot above the grass,
Which fears the keen edge of the Curtelace:
Whereof the rich Egiptian so endeares,
Root, bark, and fruit, and yet much more the tears.
There lives the sea-oake in a little shell,
There grows untilled the ruddy Cochenell,
And there the Chermez, which on each side arms
With pointed prickles all his precious arms,
Rich trees and fruitful in these worms of price,
Which pressed, yield a crimson colored juice,
When thousand lambs are died so deep in grain,
That their own mothers know them not again,
There mounts the Melt which serves in Mexico,
For weapon, wood, needle and thread to sow,
Brick, honey, sugar, sucket, balm, and wine,
Parchment, perfume, apparel, cord, and line,
His wood for fire, his harder leaves are fit,
For thousand uses of inuentiue wit.
I. Syluester. The pedant minister, and serving clarke,
The ten pound base, frize ierkin hireling,
The farmers chaplain, with his quarter warke.
The twenty noble Curate and the thing
Called Elder, all these needs will bring
All reverend titles into deadly hate
Their godly calling, and their hie estate.
Th. Storer. Of Trees.
The shady Groaues of noble palm tree spraies,
Of amorous mirtles and immortal baies.
Never vnlearn'd, but evermore there new,
Selfe-arching arms in thousand arbours grew.
I. Syluester. Rivers.
Swift Guylon, Phyton and rich Tigris tide,
And that fair stream whose silver waves do kis,
The Monarch towers of proud Semyranis.
Euprates.
Idem. Of Homer.
Sweet numbered Homer.
I. Syl. Heartstealing Homer, marrow of the Muses,
Chief grace of Greece, best pearl of Poetry,
Drowner of souls, with arts orewhelming sluices,
Embellished with Phoebes lunarie,
Decked with the graces rich imbroderie.
Sweet honey-suckle, whence all Poets sprights,
Suck the sweet honey of divine delights.
C. Fitz-Ieffrey. Of S. P. S.
Hector tongued Sydney, Englands Mars and Muse.
Idem. VVindes.
-- O heavens fresh fannes quoth he,
Earths sweeping broomes, of forests enemy:
O you my Heralds and my messengers,
My nimble posts and speedy messengers,
My arms, my sinews, and my Eagles swift,
That through the air my rolling chariot lift.
I. Syluester. The Aeolian crowde.
Idem. * O sacred Olive, firstling of the fruits,
Health-boading branch.
Idem. * The proud horse, the rough-skind Elephant,
The lusty bull, the Camel water want.
Idem. * -- Let the pearly morn
The radiant Moon, and rhumie evening see
Thy neck still yoaked with captivity.
Idem. * There natures story, till th'heauen shaker dread,
In his just wrath, the flaming sword had set,
The passage into Paradise to let.
I. Syluester. Of the infernal flood.
-- He summond up
With thundering call the damned crew, that sup
Of sulphurie Stix, and fiery Phlegeton,
Bloody Cocytus, muddy Acheron.
I. Syluester. * The sun the seasons stinter.
Idem. Of language before confusion.
-- Ah that language sweet
Sure bond of Cities friendships masticke sweet,
Strong curb of anger yerst united, now
In thousand dry brooks straies, I wot not how:
That rare rich gold, that charm grief, fancy mouer^
That calm-rage, hearts thief, quel-pride coniure-louer,
That purest coin then current in each coast
Now mingled, has sound, weight, and color lost.
It Is counterfeit, and over every shore,
The confusd fall of Babell yet does roar.
Idem. -- Then all spoke the speech
Of God himself, the old sacred Idiome rich,
Right perfect language, where is no point nor sign.
But hides some rare deep mystery behind.
Idem. Of Scaliger.
-- Scaliger our ages wonder,
The learned's sun, who eloquently can
Speak Hebrew, Greek, French, Latin, Nubian,
Dutch, Tuscan, Spanish, English, Arabicke.
The Sirian, Persian, and the Caldaike.
O rich quick spirit; O wits Chamelion,
Which any authors color can put on.
Great Julius fame, and Siluius worthy brother,
The Immortal grace of Gascony, their mother,
Idem. Wing-footed Hermes, purseuant of Jove.
Idem. Of the Hebrew tongue.
-- All hail thou sempiternall spring
Of spiritual pictures, speech of heavens hie King,
Mother and mistress of all the tongues the prime,
Which pure have past such vast deep gulphs of time,
Which have no word but ways, whose elements
Flow with hid sense, thy points with sacraments.
O sacred Dialect, in thee the names
Of men, towns, countries, register their fames
In brief abridgements: and the names of birds,
Of water guests, and forest haunting herds,
Are open brooks, where every man might read.
Miscellanea. Of the Graces. * Acidale.
-- The Graces daughters of delight,
Handmaides of Venus, which are wont to haunt
* Upon this hill, and dance there day and night,
Those three to men all gifts of graces grant,
And all that Venus in her self does vaunt,
Is borrowed of them.
Ed. Sp. They are the daughters of skie-ruling Jove,
By him begot of fair Eurynome,
The Oceans daughter in this pleasant Grove,
As he this way coming from feastfull glee
Of Thetis wedding with Aeacidee,
In summers shade himself here rested weary.
The first of them hight mild Euphrosyne,
Next fair Aglaia, last Thalia merry,
Sweet goddesses al three, which men in mirth do cherry
Therefore they always smoothly seemd to smile,
That we likewise should mild and gentle be,
And also naked are, that without guile
Or false dissemblance all them plain may see,
Simple and true, from covert malice free:
And eke themselves so in their dance they bore,
That two of them still forward seemd to be.
But one still forwards should her self before,
That good should from us go, then come in greater store.
Idem. Simoniake and unlearned Ministers.
Such men are like our curtains at their best,
To make us sleep, or hinder us from light:
Troublers of nature, children of the West,
Haters of sense, adopted sons of night,
In whom the wise both sorrow and delight.
Yet were there not such Vegetalls the while,
What had the wiser sort whereat to smile?
Th. Storer. Renowned Picus of Mirandula,
Hated the substance of a Clergy man
That was unlettered, and made a law,
An ignorant which never had began
To seek, or after seeking never scan.
Some part of somewhat that might wisdom bring,
Should be accounted but a living thing.
Idem. The noble Tichobraghe for whose dear sake
All Denmark is in admirations love:
In deep regard such difference does make
Between those men whose spirits soar above,
And those base essents which only move.
That in his Isles Horizon he admits
No cloudy Meteors of such foggy wits.
Idem. Of Beasts.
The multitude to Jove a suit imparts,
With neighing, blaying, braying, and barking,
Roaring and howling, for to have a king.
A king in language theirs they said they would,
(For then their language was a perfect speech)
The Byrds likewise which chirpes and puing could,
Cackling and chattering, that of Jove beseech,
Only the Owl still warnd them not to seech.
So hastily, that, which they would repent,
But saw they would, and he to deserts went.
Jove wisely said (for wisdom wisely says)
O Beasts take heed what you of me desire,
Rulers will think all things made them to please,
And soon forget the swinke due to their hire,
But since you will part of my heavenly fire,
I will you lend, the rest your selves must give,
That it both seen and felt may with you live.
Full glad they were, and took the naked sprite
Which strait the earth ycloathed in his clay:
The Lion heart; the Ownce gave active might.
The Horse good shape; the Sparrow lust to play,
Nightingale voice, entising songs to say.
Elephant gave a perfect memory,
And Parrot ready tongue that to apply.
The Fox gave craft; the Dog gave flattery,
Ass patience; the Mole a working thought:
^agle high thought, Wolf secret cruelty,
Monky sweet breath; the Cow her fair eyes brought
The Ermion whitest skin, spotted with naught.
The Sheep mild seeming face, climing the Bear,
The Stag did give the harme-eschuing fear.
The Hare her sleights, the Cat his melancholy,
^nt industry, and Conny skill to build:
^anes order; Storkes to be appearing holy.
^amelion ease to change, Ducke ease to yield,
^rocodile tears, which might be falsely spild.
Ape greeting gave, though he did mowing stand,
The instrument of instruments the hand.
S. Phil. Sidney. Preparations for defense.
^me built the breaches of their broken town,
that heaven and Panimire had broken down.
^me other found a Cautell against the Ram,
To save the wall unbroken where it came.
Thus Jacobs towns on all sides had their flanks
With Gabions strong, with bulwarks and with banks.
Some others busy went and came in routs,
To Terrace Towers, some under baskets louts.
Some others also wanting time and might
To strength their Towns, yet used all kind of slight
To dig up ditches deep for Cesternes good,
To draw to them the best and nearest flood.
Th. Hudson. * -- The hidden love that now adays does hold,
The steel and lodestone, Hydrargire and gold.
The Amber and straw; that lodges in one shell,
Pearle-fish and Sharpling: and unites so well
Sargons and Goats, the Sperage and the Rose,
Th'Elme and the Vine, th'Oliue and Myrtle bush
Is but a spark or shadow of that love,
Which at the first in every thing did move.
When as the earths Muses with harmonious sound,
To heavens sweet music humbly did resound.
But Adam being chief of all the strings
Of this large Lute, ore-retched, quickly brings
All out of tune: and now for melody
Of warbling charms, it yells so hideously
That it affrights fell Enynon, who turmoiles
To raise again the old Chaos anticke broiles.
I. Syl. * -- Holy Nectar that in heavenly bowers
Eternally selfe-powring Hebe powers.
Or blessed Ambrosia, Gods immortal fare.
Idem. * O who shall show the countenance and gestures
Of mercy and justice? which fair sacred sisters
With equal poize do ever balance even,
Th'vnchanging proiects of the king of heaven.
The One stern of look, the other mild aspecting,
The One pleased with tears, the other blood affecting.
The One bears the sword of vengeance unrelenting,
The Other kings pardon, for the true repenting.
The one earths Eden, Adam did dismiss,
The Other has raisde him to a higher bliss.
Idem. * Day has his golden Sun, her Moon the night,
Her fixed and wandering stars, the azure sky:
So framed all by their Creators might,
That still they live and shine, and nere shall die.
Till in a moment with the last days brand
They burn, and with them, burn earth, sea and land.
Ed. Fairfax. -- The Wolf the trembling sheep pursues,
The crowing Cock the Lion stout eschues.
The Pullaine hide them from the Puttocks flight,
The Masties mute at the Hyaenas sight.
Yea, who would think of this fell enmities?
Rage in the senseless trunks of plants and trees.
The Vine, the Cole, the Colewort Sow bread dreeds,
The Fearne abhorres the hollow wauing Reeds.
The Olive and the Oak participate,
Even to their earth, signs of their ancient hate.
Which suffers not (o date less discord) the one
Live in that ground, where the other first has grown.
I. Syl. So at the sound of Wolfe-drums rattling thunder,
The Affrighted sheep-skin drum does rent in sunder.
So that fell monsters twisted entraile cuts
By secret power the poor Lambs twined guts.
Which after death in stead of bleating mute,
Are taught to speak upon an Iuory Lute.
And so the princely Eagles ravening plumes,
The feathers of all other foul consumes.
Idem. * -- There the tree from of whose trembling top,
Both swimming Shoales and flying troops do drop.
I mean the tree now in Iuturna growing,
Whose leaves dispearst by Zephyrs wanton blowing,
Are metamorphos'd both in form and matter,
On land to fowls, to fishes on the water.
Idem. * -- The Partrich new hatched bears
On her weak back, her parents house, and wears
In stead of wings, a beuer rupple down
Follows her damme, through furrows up and down*
Idem. * -- We see the new fallen silly Lamb
Yet staind with blood of his distressed damme,
Knows well the Wolf, at whose fell sight he shakes,
And right the teate of th'vnknowne Eawe he takes.
Idem. Furies.
Alecto, sad Megera, and Thesiphon,
The nights black saunghters grim-fac'd Furies sad.
Stern Plutoes posts.
I. Syluester. Nepenthe.
Nepenthe is a drink of sovereign grace,
Devised of the Gods for to assage
Hearts grief, and bitter gall away to chase,
Which stir up anguish and contentious rage,
In stead thereof, sweet peace and quiet age,
It does establish in the troubled mind
Few men but such as sober are and sage,
Are by the Gods to drink thereof assignde,
But such as drink, eternal happiness do find.
Ed. Sp. -- Nepenthe enemy to sadness,
Repelling sorrows, and repeating gladness.
Elyxer that excells,
Save men or angels, every creature else.
I. Syl. Of Echo.
Th'aires daughter Echo haunting woods among,
A blab that will not (cannot keep her tongue)
Who never asks, but ever answers all,
Who lets not any her in vain to call.
Idem. Of the Marigold.
The Marigold so likes the lovely Sun,
That when he sets, the other hides his face:
And when he gins his morning course to run,
She spreads abroad, and shows her greatest grace.
T. Watson. Of the Eagle.
No bird but Jove's can look against the sun.
Idem. -- Enuies bird must say when all is done,
No bird but one is sacred to the sun.
Idem. Hercules Labors.
Beast, Snake, Bore, Stag, Birds, Belt, Plankes, Bull,
Thief, Fruit, Dog Diomede,
Choakt, scard, pauncht, cought, pierst, prizd,
Washed, thrown, slain, pulled, chaind, horsed.
W. Warner. Nylus.
Great Nilus land, where rain does never fall.
T. Hudson. There quakes the plant, which in Pudefetan
Is called the shamefac't, for ashamed of man,
If toward it one do approach too much,
It shrinkes the boughs, to shun our hateful touch.
As if it had a soul, a sense, and sight,
Subject to shame, fear, sorrow and despite.
I. Syluester. Of Acheron.
Rude Acheron, a loathsome lake to hell,
That boiles and bubs up swelth as black as hell.
Where grisly Charon at their fixed tide
Still ferries ghosts unto the farther side.
M. Sackuile. Echidna.
Echidna is a monster direful dread,
Whom Gods do hate and heavens abhor to see:
So hidious is her shape, so huge her head,
That even the hellish fiends affrighted be
At sight thereof, and from her presence fly.
Yet did her fair and former parts profess,
A fair young maiden, full of comely glee:
But all her hinder parts did plain express,
A monstrous Dragon, full of fearful ugliness.
Ed. Spencer. 4. Cardinal virtues.
Andronica that wisely sees before,
And Phronesis the Judge, and chaste Drucilla,
And she that boldly fights for virtues lore,
Descending from the Romine race Camilla.
S. I. Harrington. Of the Eagle.
The Cedar-building Eagle bears the wind,
And not the Falchon, though both Hawks by kind.
That Kingly bird does from the clouds command
The fearful foul that moves but nere the land.
M. Dray. Phoenix.
-- The bird of fame
That still renewes it self and never dies,
And only one in all the world there flies.
S. I. Harrington. * Of all chaste birds the Phaenix does excel,
Of all strong beasts the Lion bears the bell:
Of all sweet flowers, the Rose does sweetest smell.
Of all pure mettalls gold is only purest,
Of all the trees the Pine has highest crest.
Of all proud birds the Eagle pleases Jove,
Of pretty fowls kind Venus likes the Dove,
Of trees Minerua does the Olive move.
T. Lodge. * Who holds league with Neptune and the wind?
S. Dan. The Phaenix gazeth on the suns bright beams,
The Echinaeus swims against the streams.
R. Green. Impossibilities.
He that the number of the leaves could cast,
That in November falls by winters blast:
He that could tell the drops of rain and sleete,
That Hyad, Orion, or Pleyiades weete.
Sheds on the ground that man might only tell,
What tears from Iudiths eyes incestant fell.
Th. Hudson. -- Like Coruiue who forgate
His Proper name; or like George Trapezunce,
Learned in youth, and in his age a dunce.
I. Syl. The firmament shall retrograde his course,
Swift Euphrates go hide him in his sourse:
Firm mountains skip like lambs beneath the deep,
Eagles shall diue, whales in the air shall keep.
Ere I presume with fingers end to touch,
Much less with lips the fruit forbid too much.
I. Syluester. Fly from thy channel, Thames forsake thy streams,
Leave the Adamant Iron, Phoebus lay thy beams.
Cease heavenly spheres, at last your watrie warke
Betray your charge, return to Chaos dark.
At least some ruthlesse Tigre hang her whelpe,
My Catisbye so with some excuse to help,
M. M. Ceston.
That girdle gave the virtue of chaste love,
And wiuehood true to all that did it bear:
But whosoever contrary does prove,
Might not the same about her middle wear,
But it would loose or else asunder tear,
Whilom it was (as Faieries wont report)
Dame Venus girdle by her esteemd dear
What time she used to live in wiuely sort.
But laid aside when so she used her sport.
Her husband Vulcan whilome for her sake,
When first he loved her with heart entire,
This precious ornament they say did make.
And wrought in Lemnos with unquenched fire,
And afterward did for her first loves hire,
Give it to her for ever to remain,
Therewith to bind lascivious desire,
And loose affections straightly to restrain,
Which virtue it for ever did retain.
This goodly Belt, was Ceston called by name.
Ed. Spencer. * The noble Lion never slaies the least,
But always prays upon some worthy beast.
The thunder throws his sulphured shafts adowne,
On Atlas high, or cold Riphins crown.
The tempest fell more fervently does fall
On houses high, then on the homely hall.
Th. Hudson. *Saturn taught men untaught before, to ear the lusty land,
And how to pierce the pathlesse air with shafts from bowmans hand.
God Dis did quail to see his gold so fast conuaid from hell,
And fishes quakt, when men in ships amidst their floods did dwell.
VV. Warner. Twelve foul faults.
A wise man living like a drone, an old man not devout,
Youth disobedient, rich men that are charity without:
A shameless woman, vicious Lords, a poor man proudly stout.
Co^tentious Christians, Pastors, that their functio^s do neglect,
A wicked King, no discipline, no laws men to direct,
Are twelve the foulest faults that most all commonwealths infect.
W. Warner. Engines of war.
-- The Inginers have the Trepan dressed,
And reared up the Ram for battery best.
Here bends the Briccoll while the Cable crackes,
There Crosbowes were vprent with iron Rackes.
Here crooked Coruies fleing Bridges tall,
Their scathfull Scorpions that ruins the wall,
On every side they raise with ioynture meet,
The timber Towers for to command the street.
The painful Pioners wrought against their will,
With fleakes and Faggots, ditches up to fill.
Th. Hudson. * -- The happy Arabs those that builds
In thatched Wagons wandering through the fields.
The subtle Tirians they who first were Clerks,
That staid the wandering words in leaves and barks.
Idem. * At Babell first confused toongs of every language grew.
W. Warner. -- Idolatry did grow
* From Ninus first, he first a Monarchy did frame.
Idem. *Lord Dane the same was called the^, to the^ a pleasing name,
Now odiously Lordan say we, when idle mates we blame.
Idem. * The Turtle that is true and chaste in love,
Shows by her mate something the spirit does move.
The Arabian byrd that never is but one,
Is only chaste because she is alone.
But had our mother Nature made them two,
They would have done as Doves and Sparrowes do.
But therefore made a Martyr in desire,
And does her penance lastly in the fire.
M. Drayton. *I cast not with fools, suffer Saints, let mighty fools be mad,
Note, Seneca by news done for precepts, penance had.
VV. Warner. * The Roman widow did when she beheld
Her son, whom erst she counted slain in feeld.
G. Gascoigne. Rivers.
Fair Danubie is praisde for being wide,
Nilus commended for the seuen-fold head:
Euphrates for the swiftness of the tide,
And for the garden whence his course is led,
The banks of Rhine with Vines are ouerspred.
Take Loyre and Po, yet all may not compare
With English Thamesis for buildings rare.
Th. Storer. FINIS.
FAmes windy trump blew up this haughty mind
To do or wish, to do what here you find:
It Was never held error yet in errant Knights
(Which privilege he claims) to dress their fights
In high hyperbolies: for youths example,
To make their minds, as they grow men, grow ample.
Thus such achievements are assaid and done
As pass the common power and sense of man.
Then let high spirits strive to imitate,
Not what he did, but what he does relate.
